URUGUAY
APPRAISAL OF FOURTH LIVESTOCK DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
I.        The proposed loan (two stages) of US$25.6 million would support a
Fourth Livestock Development Project financed by the Bank and would continue
technical and financial assistance for Uruguay's national program of live-
stock development for a two-year period. The first :.oan of US$7.0 million
was made in 1959 (245-UR). Subsequent loans were made of US$12.7 million
(1965 -- 407-UR), and US$6.3 million (1970 -- 698-UR), the latter being
supplemented by US$4.0 million in June 1971 (773-UR).
ii.       A new technology of pasture improvement was introduced through
the first two loans. Despite good technical progresE, 'however, a broad
sector impact was not realized because of adverse government policies
affecting pricing, credit, processing, and marketing.
iii.      Since June 1969, the Bank and the Governmert of Uruguay have
pursued a dialogue on livestock sector policy improvements and reorganiza-
tion of Project on-lending arrangements. During this period, the Govern-
ment took a series of difficult policy decisions which, supported by a
rise in export prices, brought a record demand for credit in 1971. These
decisions led to more efficient operation of packing plants, improved
meat inspection and hygiene control, tax and pricing measures to encourage
exports and restrain domestic consumption, and a broadening of the banking
base for channelling development credit to the livestock sector.
{v.       The results have been impressive.  Meat exports in 1970 reached
145,000 m tons worth a record US$88 million, compared to exports of
69,000 m tons valued at US$27 million in 1961. Meat processors invested
some US$8 million in modernizing 15 plants in 1969 and 1970. Since the
livestock sector contributes approximately 85% of Uruguay's exports and
30% to GNP, maintenance of sound sector policies and provision of adequate
investment incentives should contribute greatly to continued real growth
in the economy.
v.        The proposed Project would continue Bank su:3port for on-farm
development, pasture seed production and processing, as well as technical
services to ranchers and meat and dairy processors. A new feature would
be to support pig (for local consumption) and dairy beef (for export)
producers and also provision for financing heifer purchase and retention
on ranches. Project funds woulad continue to be channc2ll1d to farmers
through participating banks from the Livestock Fund administered by the
Central Bank.
vi.       All development loans to Project ranchérs would be based
on technically sound and financially viable ranch devclonment plans. About
3,600 farmers would receive loans each year averaging about U'S$3,600 per



- ii -
loan. All Uivestock farmers would be eligible to bcrrow under the Project
and participating farms would range in size from. 30 ha for pigs, to 4,000 ha
for beef catt:Le and sheep production. The average borrower would have a
holding of approximately 670 ha. As in Loan 698-UR subloans would be indexel!
tc cover possible future inflation. Overall Project administration would
continue to be handled by the Plan Agropecuario whose competent technicians
would assist ranchers ir. preparing their plans. The Bank's loan disburse-
ments amounted to US$9.2 million for calendar year 1971.
vii.      International competitive bidding would be used for the bulk
procurement of pasture seeds, fertilizer and machinery for seed producers.
Normal coimercial channels would be used for the purchase of other inputs
such as fencing, water point materials, and building supplies.  These
procedures would be the same as under the previous two loans.
viii.     The econonic rate of return on the investment in this Project
is estimated to be 37%2, while the financial rate of return to participating
farmers varies from 13% to 30%, depending on the type of farm.  At full
development, after seven years, the investments are likely to result in
an incremental beef production of 49,000 m tons, valued at US$34 million
annually on the export market.
ix.       Total Project cost is estimated at US$56 million, of which 46%
would be met by the Bank, 7% by Government, 13% by the Central Bank, 18% by
participating banks, and 16% oy farmers. The Project is suitable for a loan
of US$25.6 million, the estimated foreign exchange component. For country
reasons, Barnk finance would be provided in two stages: a first loan of
US$11.2 million would be made in FY 72 to cover the first year program; a
second loan of US$14.4 million would be made ir. FY 73. Both loans would be
based on thi4s appraisal report. However, there would be an updating of
information contained in this report prior to submitting the second loan to
the Board.  The bor-ower would be the Governuient of Uruguay, which would bear
the foreign exchange risk. The loan term would be 14 years, including a
4-year grace period.



URUGUAY
APPRAISAL OF FOURTH LIVESTOCK DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      Uruguay has applied for a Bank loan to assist in financing the
Fourth Project of its livestock development program with which the Bank
has been closely associated for more than 20 years. In 1950 a joint FAO-Bank
mission advised the Government on a pilot scheme _o test new pasture
improvement techniques. These experiments, carried out on about 120,000
ha belonging to various commercial ranches, proved successful and laid the
foundation for the subsequent Bank livestock lending program. In 1959,
the First Loan of US$7 million (245-UR) was grantad; six years later, in
1965, a Second Loan of US$12.7 million (407-UR) was approved. In 1970, a
Third Loan for US$6.3 million (698-UR) was signed and was supplemented in June
1971 by US$4 million (773-UR).
1.02      A Bank appraisal mission to Uruguay in June 1969 reviewed in
depth the impact of previous lending and concluded that an excellent tech.nical
base had been established. It found, however, that a number of sector policy
improvements (marketing, processing, pricing, tax, meat hygiene and credit)
would be required if this base was to be broadened into a national livestock
development program. Many of the changes recommeended by the mission were
carried out and the stimulus given to the Livestock sector contributed much
to the growth of the economy in 1969 and 1970.
1.03      Since mid-1970, Uruguay's livestock industrv has been making
rapid progress. Subloans under the Plan Agropecuatrio (PLAN) program in
1971 have been nearly triple (US$11 million) the average rate of lending
in recent years of about US$4 million. The raeat processing industry
invested 3S$8 million in 1969 and 1970 for modern:zazion and intends to
keep up the pace with the assistance of an Inter-t.merican Development Bank
loan of US$13.7 million, signed in March 197i. 1, A high rate of invest-
ment in t'he vital livestock sector must be sustained if it is to develop
and contribute effectively to the country's economic growth.
1.04      This report is based on the findings of a June 1971 appraisal
mission, comprising Messrs. A. Schumacher and E.P. Schertz (Bank), and
E.A. Clarke and J. Romero (Consultants).
1/   Other international and bilateral aid ag;encies continue to be active
in support of Uruguav's agricultural developrent. By 1971 these
operations had amounted to a total of USS71.9 million. A breakdowrn
of the principal aid agencies and operations is given in Annex 1,
Table 6.



- 2 -
II. BAC(IGROUND
A. Agriculture and Livestock in the Economy
2.01      Agriculture contributes, directly and indirectly, about 30% to
Uruguay's GNP and employs 19% (191,000) of the total labor force. Exports
of livestock products (US$191 million in 1970) account for about 85% of
export earnings.  Crop production contributes only 25% as much as livestock
to GNP and most crops - wheat, rice, sorghum, fruits, and vegetables - are
growa to supply local needs only. Livestock production from grass is the
mainstay of the agriculture sector.  Uruguay has a marked comparative
advantage in the production of beef cattle, sheep and milk.  Expansion of
wool production and dairying is difficult due to limited international
prospects for these two products. Government has therefore concentrated on
measures to increase beef production for export. The possibilities for
producing alternative agriculture products for export are limited. There
are some 58,000 farms in the country. Since 1948, when 38 farmers were
settled on 9,000 ha, the Institute of National Colonization (INC) has
settled more than 1,000 farmers on 250,000 ha. Uruguayan law provides that
any farm or ranch up for sale must first be offered to INC which has first
right to purchase at the going market rate. Overall, there has been
relatively little pressure for land, especially as soils and climate are
better suited to pastoral than crop production. Further details on the
agricultural and livestock sectors are provided in Annex 1.
Livestock Production Patterns
2.02      Uruguay, with an agricultural area of 16.5 million ha, of which
about 15 million ha are used for livestock, has a gently undulating topogra-
phy with virtually no wasteland. Pastures support 8.5 million beef cattle,
mainly Hereford; 600,000 dairy cattle, mainly Friesian; a sheep flock of
19.8 million, mainly Corriedale; and 380,000 Landrace and Duroc pigs. As
the country can no longer bring large areas of new land into production,
increased livestock output must come principally from increased production
per hectare.
2.03      Uruguay continues to be a major international supplier of live-
stock products, exporting US$191 million worth in 1970: US$79 million of
beef, US$9 million of mutton and lamb, US$79 million of wool, and US$24 mil-
lion of hides and skins.  Cattle production, stagnating since the early
1950's, began to increase in the late 1960's. Wool and dairy production
have remained virtually at a standstill.
2.04      Beef exports have exceeded 100,000 m tons for three successive
years -- 112,000 m tons in 1968, 124,000 m tons in 1969, and 145,000 m tons
in 1970. This was accomplished despite import bans imposed by the United
Kingdom in 1969 and later by the United States because of packing plant
sanitary deficiencies. The USA ban has since been lifted and the UK ban



- 3 -
is currently under review. Most of the Additional beef has been exported to
Eastern Europe and the Mediterranean countries. Preliminary results from
the 1970 census indicate that the national beef herd has increased about
4% in numbers since 1966. This expansion, particularly in breediag stock,
ust be accelerated if recent exports are to be sustained.
B. Agricultural Services
2.05      The Ministry of Agriculture and Livestock is responsible for the
technical development of the livestock subsector. Under its auspices,
the Honorary Commission of the PLAN, organized in 1959, is charged with
providing technical services to cattle and sheep ranchers as well as to
dairy and pig producers (Annex 2 provides further details).
Research
2.06      Agricultural research, at the National Agricultural Research
Center (La Estanzuela), has been assisted by several UNDP/FA0 specialists
for many years. Research has been conducted on crops, pastures, soil
fertility, animal production, and agricultural economics. The Research
Center also has organized a seed certification program to upgrade produc-
tion of legumes, grasses, and forages.  Veterinarry research is conducted
at the Rubino Laboratories near Montevideo. Because of the importance of
livestock to the UruguAyan economy, more research and diagnostic facilities
have been planned.  The Inter-American Development- Bank (IDB) has loaned
US$1.3 million to the Government to help control foot and mouth disease
(FHD), which has been endemic in Uruguay for many years. Commercial FMN
vaccine was first produced locally in 1955 and the vaccination of all
cattle has recently become a legal requirement. :ct is hoped that this
program will help the retention of markets in many European countries.
The Rubino Laboratories have also developed and marketed the strains of
rhizobium inoculum used in Uruguay.
2.07      Inadequate management and nutrition, rather than disease, are
the main obstacles to higher levels of production. in spring and autumn
feed supplies are plentiful, but in sumer and winter their qcuantity and
quality are wuch less. Because ranchers do little to overcome these
seasonal shortages, calving and lambing percentages tend to be :ow and
mortality rates high. Given the high genetic quality of stock (para 2.02),
production on non PLAN ranches is below potential. For example, weaning
rates for Hereford and Angus cattle in Europe and the U.S. commonly average
above 80Z, whereas in Uruguay the same breeds rarely exceed 55% on unim-
proved ranches. The PLAN has fostered better pasture management and
nutritional levels, both by improving pastures anc. forage conservation.
Pasture growth is thereby transferred fram the surplus spring and fall
to the deficit summer and winter periods.



-4-
Agricultural Credit
2.08      The ava:ilbility of short-term credit to ranchers and farmers has
beean uncertain. In 1967 and 1968 a strong credit squeeze was imposed to
help contain a runaway unflation which, for a tine in 1967, reached an
annual rate of 120%. Short-term credit, in real terms, to agriculture was
reduced by 50Z, and this was partially responsible for a fall in demand for
PLAN development credit. Restoration of short-term (6-18 months) agricultural
credit availability, though atill insufficient, has been partly responsible
for the increase in PLAN long-term loan demand. As additional pasture is
improved, the provision of short-term credit and permanent capital on a
regular and increasing basis to participating ranchers and farmers vlli be
required.
2.09      The Government-owned Bank of the Republic (BOR) has provided
virtually all of the long-term development credit and much of the short
term credit to the agricultural sector, with comrercial banks (32 in
Uruguay) also providiug short-term credit. The BOR, with 71 branches in
the agricultural regions, accounts for over 50% of all agricultural credit
outstanding, both seasonal and developmental. About half of the agricultural
portfolio of government and private banks has been discounted with the
Central Bank.
2.10      Interest rates vary greatly on agricultural and industrial
loans. Commercial rates to agriculture and to industry on short-term loans
are fixed at 24% per annum but can range up to 40% when various commissions
and charges have bean added. The official short- and medium-term agricultural
credit by the BOR Costs borrowers about 13%. With inflation averaging 20-25%
in recent years, this rate offers a substantial subsidy.
C. Livestock Development 2olicies and Programs
2.11      During the early 1960's, a series of fiscal policies were
formulated tlAt heavixly penalized livestock production, processing and
exports and led to stagnation in the industry up to 1968. These policies
included: (a) heavy export taxes on beef and wool; (b) high sales taxes
on all cattle and wool sold; (c) heavy import duties on agricultural
machinery; and (d) credit restrictions that bore especially hard on
agriculture.
2.12      The positive technical achievements in pasture improvement and
increased carrying capacity under Loans 245-UR and 407-UR were offset by
these policies. Without their modification, further financial support
for investments in primary production would have been largely pointless.
Moreover, development needed to extend beyond production and include
1/   Further details of agricultural credit services and facilities are set
out in Annex 3.



- 5 -
processing and marketing. Remedial Government action was required in two
areas before the Bank could continue financial support of the livestock
development program. Fiscal measures had to be modified to restore investment
incentives to livestock producers; and the marketing and meat packing
industry had to be modernized to improve its cost structure, and the meat
inspection service had to be upgraded to international standards.
2.13      Since mid-1969, the Bank and Government have pursued a dialogue
directed at improving policies and the overall investment climate in the
livestock sector. The results of the policy changes outlined below have
exceeded expectations -- both for the rancher and for the economy. These
policies enabled full advantage to be taken of the substantial increases in
world prices which followed in 1970.
2.14      Prior to this sharp increase in world market prices Government had
taken the following steps aimed at improving producers' investment incentives:
(a) Beef export taxes were changed from an ad valorem tax of 15% to a flat
rate of US$40 1/ per ton of beef exported (equivalent to 8o% ad valorem), in-
ducing meat packers to export higher quality, more processed cuts of beef,
(b) wool export taxes were reduced from 29% to 5%; (c) import duties and
surcharges on farm machinery were abolished; and (d) ranch-gate price cor.-
trols on beef cattle were abolished. These measures resulted in a 40% ir.-
crease in the consumer price of beef, helping reduce domestic consumption
in favor of exports.
2.15      To stimulate the meat packing industry, the following measures
were taken:
(a) the 40-year old Frigonal (Government-owned packing
house) monopoly over the Montevideo market was
eliminated. All meat packing plants caa now supply
this domestic market (the monopoly was primarily
responsible for the excessive slaughtering charges
in the meat packing industry);
(b) the inefficiencies of the publicly controlled packing
houses were reduced;
(c) the meat inspection service was considerably restruc-
tured, expanded, and improved; anc
(d) a modernization program, supported. by aa !DB Loan of
US$13.7 million, was prepared and is now being
executed.
1/   This has since been raised to US$80 per ton but, because international
prices have increased markedly, the overall effect has been that the
tax levied is equivalent to about 10% ad valorem.

IrI. IMPACT OP BANIC LENDING
A. New Technology
3.01      Under the Bank lending program, new technologies of legume/phos-
phate pasture izaprovement were succeasfully introduced. Initially, much
reliance was placed on the slow, erpensive but essentially reliable method
that in lved conventional soving of mixed grasses and legumes on a
prepared seed bed. This method, preferred on the deep fertile soils,
has been supplemnted by an inexpensive technique, especially on the shallow
soils, involving the extensive use of the Australian sodseeder, and also by
dropping pelleted seed and fertilizer from special airplanes. 1/
3.02      By 1965, these and other techniques had been evaluated and the
PLAN knew, more or lesis, which one was preferable under the varied ecolo-
gical conditions around the country. The inexpensive pasture improvement
techniques became a demanstrated success, particularly in ensuring
pasture availability iln the winter and more persistent growth into the
hot suer dry spells.
B. Experimentation
3.03      Phosphate deficiency was shown early to be a key limiting factor
in the establishment of improved legume-based pastures, as was the lack
of suitable strains of rnizobia inoculum. Under the guidance of an
FAO-supported program, specific rhizobia strains were prepared and widely
used with outstanding success.
3.04      While experimentation had been successful, farmers were slow to
appreciate ;he profitability of improved pastures. Recently, however, as
a result of clear demonstration and intensive extension activities,
together with a rationalization of economic policies (para 2.14), farmers
have become more convinced of the benefits of the PLAN introduced technology.
The average annual Tate of grass lanc  improvement has increased from
46,000 ha during the first five years of the program to 230,000 ha for
the years 1969 through 1971.
1/  Nine topdressirig and oversowing planes and four bulk handling trucks
have been imported through the PLAN. In 1970, over 30,000 ha were
sown by pianes.



C. On-Rmch Production Impact
3.05      During the early 1960's, the PLAN moved slowly as experience
was gained with the introduction of new technology. Since 196f;, the annual
volume of lending has almost tripled -- from US$4 million equivalent to
an estimated US$11 million in 1971. Further acceleration of PLAN
lending and technical activities is expected over the next five years
for the improvement of an additional 20% (currently 7%) of Uruguay's
pasture area.
3.06      A sample survey of 50 representative properties indicates that
output can be raised from the equivalent of about 70 kg (beef liveweight)
per ha on the unimproved natural pastures to an average of some 220 kglha --
a threefold increase. The best pastures, in the more favored ecological
zones and under good management, have greatly exceeded this output and
their permanence has been clearly established. A wider sample survey of
250 farms is currently under way to establish further details, particularly
the numbers of breeding cattle on PLAN supported farms.
3.07      Pasture improvement plans have now been executed on about 6,100
supervised farms throughout the country, and by the end of 1971 an
estimated 830,000 ha will have been improved. Ranchers outside the Project,
who were supplied seed and fertilizer and techr.ical assistance on a cash
basis, have developed another 200,000 ha. Under the First Project, farm
size averaged 600 ha, compared to the 1 ,000 ha units envisaged. During
the Second Project, ranch size dropped to about 500 ha, due to the strict
interpretation by BOR of ceiling regulations or. capital resources of
eligible Project ranchers. The average area of participating project
ranches is now about 700 ha, with about 100 ha of improved pasture per
ranch.
D. Loan Repayments
3.08      The repayment record of Project 'borrowers under prevSIous Bank
sponsored projects in 1969, 1970 and 1971 hax baen good. .n 1969, interest
payment3 to BOR on adjusted outstanding principal  were good. In 1970,
about 5% of amounts due for both indexed and uninrexed loans were 3 month&
overdue. Ag of June 1971, about 3% of the indeAed portfolio was overdue up
to 6 mths and 2% overdue beyond one year.
E. Other Benefits
3.09      The Bank's livestock lending program has had a nuber of important
further benefits, such as:



(a)  Limt kic ect . 3ldcy. For t' e first time, Government
policynakers ail- treating the livestock industry, rancher,
and processor, as an integrated sector.  The PLAN has
played arn important coordinating role in this new approach.
Details of sector policy improvements are provided in
paras 2.14 and 2.15, and Annex 1.
(b)  Export Policy.  The approach has helped ensure that the
extra beef production is channelled into export markets,
resulting in adcIt ional foreign exchange earnings of
approximately .S$50 miflion from an additional 84,000
m tons in 1970 compared to 1959.
(c)  Rural DevelopmivnQ aad Land Refora.  The PLAN program has
directly assisted the land reform efforts of the National
Institute of Colonization (INC). Of the more than 1.,0
farmers and ranchers settled by the INC, 178 have received
PLAIN credit support.
(d)  isecolo.  The  LAN projects, by introducing soil
improving leguie-baaed pasture, have begun to reverse
the decline in soil fertility, the result of years of
continuous cropping and grazing. Erosion control measures
have a-so been intensified with PLAN technical support.
Project funds have been used to finance consultant
specialists who made a series of recomendations, many
of which have been adopted, to improve both the hygiene
conditions in meat planta and their processing of
wastes which had been previously disposed of as raw
effluent.
(e)  EMPoymnta.  Some 11,000 individuals are currently employed
on the Project ranches, and another 4,000 are employed in
the rural meat packing works.  Although the proposed Project
would have limited impact on increasing this figure, it
should ensure them steady employment and income.
(f) Marketing. UJnder the Third Project efforts were intensified
to improve the interritiornal marketing of beef. A major
study of Uruguay's external beef markets is being conducted
by a Swiss firn- which was selected after international
bidding. The sane group would, fo'lowing this market study,
suggest improvements in the flow o£ forei6,n market informa-
tion to the 18 exporting packing houses.  In acdition, the
Project financed a group of six Uruguayan officials to make
a detailed study of hygiene and plant efficiencies in
competitor nations such as New Zealand, Australia, Yugoslavia,
Denmark, and Ireland.

IV. THE FOURTH LIVESTOCK DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
A. Definition
4.01      The proposed Project would continue to assist Uruguayis livestock
development program by providing technical and financial support to beef
cattle, sheep, pig and dairy beef producers for calendar years 1972 and
1973. In previous loans beef cattle and sheep production was supported.
This project includes provision for dairy beef and pig production subcompo-
nents. It would embrace all livestock areas in the country. Expansion of
the pasture seed production and processing facilities would be included, as
well as support for Project-related intensified pasture and livestock re-
search programs.  Technical services to ranchers, to the meat and dairy
processing sector, and to Uruguay's meat inspection and hygiene control service
would also be provided.
B. Detailed Features
4.02      Beef Cattla and Sheep.  The Project woulc. finance on-ranch
development plans for about 3,100 ranches in each of zwo years. The objec-
tive would be to continue the improvement of cattle and sheep breeding per-
formance, to achieve earlier slaughter ages, and tc shift the emphasis
from wool production to mutton and lamb. Investments would be mainly in
pasture improvement; breeding stock; fencing; machinery; water supplies;
and other ranch infrastructure. The average '.nvestment per ranch would be
around US$6,000 equivalent, and the average ranch size 770 ha.
4.03      The ProJect would include financing for both the purchase of
additional breeding heifers and retention of breeding heifers from the
rancher's own herd. Provision for financing heifer purchase would be
included in the development loans to ranchers, which would finance 80% of
the investment costs stated in the approved ranch plans. The development
loans would range from 7 to 10 years. To enable ranchers to retain heifers
from their own herd, loans would be made to provide them with incremental
working capital with the loan term ranging from 3 to 7 years. This term is
governed by the time required to generate offtaie and the need to retain
additional heifers for breeding for a number of years to insure pasture
utilization. The interest rate would be 11% on boti the development loans
and the loans for incremental working capital. Under previous Bank loans
heifer purchases were excluded because the additional forage provided by
an estimated 10% improved pasture could be utilized well within the biolo-
gical growth of a well managed breeding herd, Now, however, many ranchers
are investing in pasture improvement on areas well in excess of 10% of
their land. This requires more investment frao the ranchers' own
resources as well as additional working capital and necessitates the
purchase of breeding stock as the retention of indigernous stock is insuffi-
cient to utilize the extra improved grassland.



- 10 -
4.04      The Project? would accelerate the growth of ranchers' breeding
herds by providing funds for heifer purchases and retentions. Careful
but different criteria would be established for purchase of additional
heifers and for heifer retention. Loans for heifer purchase would only
be made if t^ae ranch or farm has more than 20% of the pasture area improved
and the rancher is practicing good herd and pasture management. Loans for
incremental workiing capital to enable heifer retention would also be subject
to these criteria but in addition, would be based on the need for such
working capital as demonstrated in the analysis of the farm budgets approved
by the PLAN. A further requirement would be that all retained heifers be
identified bJ the PLAN. All heifer loans would be approved by the Technical
Director. Assurances on this and the enforcement of these criteria were
obtained during negotiations. Terms and conditions of the loans for the
purchase and retention of heifers are further discussed in Aex  3, Appendix
2. Details of three types of beef and sheep ranches which would be financed
under the Project are given in Annex 5, Tables 1-24.
4.05      Dairy Beef.  Ivestments in dairy beef production are expected
mostly in the south and southwest areas (Map 1).  Some 900 farmers, averaging
about 100 ha each, are expected to receive financial and technical support
over a two-year period, 450 in each year. The objective is to raise
20,000 male calves, currently killed at birth, to 14 to 18 months of age
for slaughte:: and export as baby beef. Average investment per farm would
be around US$3,000 equivalent, for pasture improvement, pens, fencing,
machinery, and ranch infrastructure. Further details are provided in Annex 6.
4.06      Pork Product±on.  The Project would support  the establishment of
200 units of about 25 breeding sows each, probably in the south-central
area of Uruguay (Map 1).  Average investments per 30 ha farm are estimated
at around US$14,000 equivalent, of which US$2,800 would be for the
important start-up feed costs.  The large initial investment is necessary
due to the capital costs of farrowing and finishing pens. Other invest-
ments would include breeding pens, feed mixing machinery, boars and sows.,
fencing, and pasture improvement. Annex 7 provides further details.
40 * O '  Contractor Machineiy. Limited funds (US$242,000) would be
provided under the Project for making loans to contractors for the purchase
of miachinery to construct on-ranch water supplies and to lay down the
different types of pasture. About 100 loans are expected to be made for
tractors, harrows, bulk fertillzer distributors , sodseeders, and farm
dam construction equipment. Such loans would be made on the basis of.
investment p.ans approved by the PLAN.  Annex 3, Appendix 2 gives further
details.
4.08      Seed Production and Processing.  The Project would include funds
for loans to farmers and seed processors for the importation of special
types of pasture seed producing, harvesting, cleaning, processing, and
certification machinery and equipment. Any financial. support to seed
processors would be based on a detailed technical and financial plan.  As-
surances were obtained that no such plans would be financed without PLAN
and Bank approval. Further details are given in Annex 4.



- 11 -
4.09      Technical Services and Training.  The Project would support
technical services for the continued introduction of new technology in
livestock production, pasture production and management, demonstration of
related pasture and forage conservation techniques, meat and dairy
processing and related marketing problems, and up-to-date hygiene
techniques. Related goods and equipment to ensure the effective utiliza-
tion of these services would also be provided. Additionally, the Project
would finance local and overseas training of livestock, meat processing,
and banking and credit technicians. Local training is being undertaken
by the Technical Director recruited from New Zealand and a Senior Agricul-
tural Economist from the Federal Republic of Germany, both positions subject
to Bank approval as in previous loans and costs would continue to be met by
the Project (para 4.21, Annex 2, Table 1). Finally, the Project would give
additional emphasis to the extension of appropriate new technologies of hay
and forage conservation, and provide a small amount for financing preinvest-
ment studies. Annex 2 gives further background on the Project's Technical
Services.

4.11      The foreign exchange component for physical investments was
computed by adding to CIF costs the additional. foreign expenditure incurred
in processing, transporting inputs to farms, and sowing or constructing
the financed investments (for example, pasture improvement and dams). Breed-
ing heifers eligible for financing under the proposed modification would be
withheld from slaughter. To the extent that such heifers would not enter the
export trade but would be retainedJ the foreign exchange earnings foregone
would represent foreigxi exchange investments in the expansion of the national
herd. Currently 40% of Uruguay's beef production is exported. For purposes
of determining the Bank's contribution to such financing it has been assumed
that the same proportion of heifers purchased and/or retained under the
Project would be withheld from exports, even though, it could be argued that
most of the future increiental production would be marketed abroad. Tech-
nical, consultant and training services and related equipment were computed
on a CIF basis.

4.15       Government's policy since the early 3960's has been to subsidize
phosphate fertiiizer costs from the earmarked proceeds of beef and wool
export taxes. This subsidy has effectively reduced farmgate costs from
US$60 to US$40 per m ton, or to about the levels of its major competitors
in New Zealand and Australia.  Compfred to New Zealard's consumption of
t,100,000 tons Jn 1970, Uruguay's consumption was only 210,000 m tons.
To encourage a wider aad more regular use of phosphate fertilizer, continued
subsidies would be approprItate until at least 20% of Uruguay's pasture area
is improved.  This level of pasture improvement should generate sufficient
farm income to allow pasture development to continue without subsidizing
phosphate. In addition at this level of usage, the value of routine applica-
tion of phosphate in maintaining pasture production will have been amply
demonstrated.  Furthermore, although subsidizing fertilizer can be justified
at low levels of usage, the cost of the subsidy becomes very burdensome to
the Government budget when fertilizer use becomes wic:espread. During



- 15 -
negotiations, assurances were obtained that the borrower would submit a plan
to the Bank by June 1, 1973 for phasing out fertilizer subsidies once 20%
of the total pasture area has been approved.
E. Procurement
4.16      International competitive bidding procedures would be used for the
bulk procurement of pasture seeds (US$2.0 million), fertilizer (US$4.0 mil-
lion), and machinery for seed producers and contractors (US$0.47 milliorn),
except for contracts valued at less than US$25,000. International bidding
for rock phosphate for local grinding and direct application would be restricte-
to those sources agronomically tested under Uruguayan conditions at the time
of bidding. To date, sources in Tunisia and Israel have been successfully
tested and a source in the U.S. is currently under test.  The PLAN is inviting
other potential suppliers to submit samples for zesting by the Estenzuela Re-
search Station. Bidding for all other types of rock phosphate would be open
to all suppliers.  Bank approval of terms of reference and draft contracts
would be required for any technical services and training programs valued at
more than US$25,000. Related equipment and goods to support technical ser-
vices (about US$200,000 for vehicles and spare parts equipment, office
machinery and supplies) would be subject to international competitive bid-
ding, except for contracts below US$25,000. Purchases of fencing and hand-
ling yard materials, farm machinery, water pumps and piping and building
materials would be made through normal commercial channels. An adequate
selection of international suppliers is represented in the local market.
These procedures are the same as were followed under Loans 698-UR and 773-UR.
Assurances on these procurement procedures were obtained during negotia-
tions.
F. Disbursement
4.17      Since the Bank would disburse directly to suppliers for importe.d
seeds and fertilizers (para 4.13), an equivalent amount of local currency
would be deposited with the Livestock Fund of the Central Bank for making
on-farm development loans under the Project. Since these funds would not
cover'the full foreign exchange cost of on-farm development loans, the Bank
would also disburse to the Livestock Fund the equ:.?valent of. 46% of on-farm
development loans made by participating banks.  The 3ank would also disburse
the full foreign exchange cost of contractor mach:.nery; seed processing equip-
ment; technical services and related equipment; ard 37% of incremental
working capital loans. Disbursements would be made against appropriate docu-
mentation submitted by the Central Bank to the Bank. Certification by the
participating banks, the Central Bank and the PLAN Technical Director wouid be
required for reimbursemenrt of on-farm development and incremental working
capital subloans. Disbursement of the loan (both stages) is expected with-in
three years (Annex 10). Appropriate assurances were obtained during nego-
tiations.

698-UR, would continue to be used as a channel for Pro4-ect funds.  On-ranch

each participating bankl would establish a separate account to handle Project
loans. That these accounts would be established in a manner satisfactory to

4.20       The PLAN would continue to coordinate and supervise the livestock
credit program (Annex 2). The Government would provide the necessary funds
for the administrative costs of the PLAN (US$380,000 annually) and assurances
were obtained during negotiations that these would be made available as
and when required.  The basic law establishing the PLAN specified that it
has technical autonomy with regard to directing and supervising the livestock
development program.  About 80 university graduates are now employed as
technicians to appraise rancher loan applications, supervise the disbursement
of approved loans and provice technical advice on techniques of pasture im-
provement.
4.21       The PLAN has made fruitful use of four experienced Technical Di-
rectors over the past 10 years. Due to the importance of this position, any
new appointment would require the approval of the Bank, as would the conditions
of his employment and the authorities, duties, and responsibilities assigned
(see Annex 2). Assurances that such approvals as under previots Projects
were obtained during negotiations. The Technical Director would be assisted
by a Deputy, Regional Supervisors and a Senior Agricultural Economist. The
principal deficiency In the planning work to date has been a lack of famil-
iarity with farm management and budgeting techniques. To rectify this, the
Senior Agricultural Economist has organized a special group to introduce
these techniques (Annex 2). Assurances were obtained that intensified on-
,arm record keeping would boe undertaken under the direction of the Senior
Agricultural Econo=,st, His foreign exchange costs would continue to be
financed bY the Loan and a-n  new appointment would be subject to Bank approval.

I. Lending Operations
4.22       The Government would be the borrower a-nd bear the foreign exchange
risk. A substantial portion of this risk would be covered by the indexed
subloans repaid by Project ranchers (para 4.24).  The Bank loan term would
be for 14 years, including a 4-year grace period. Lending operations and
policies are suimarized briefly below; details are given in Annex 3, page 5.
4.23       Additional Lending Channels.  In Decemoer 1970, to help create
healthy competition for on-lending, the Central Bank notified the banking
system that all banks were eligible to be considered for PLAN participation.
Eligibility criteria are given in Annex 3. Additional banking participation
would be encouraged by the higher (85% average) reimbursement rate (para 4.14)i
the graduated scale of reimbursement and discount rates (Annex 3), the provi-
sion for discounting incremental working capital loans and the detailed farm
budgeting required under the Project. Assurances were obtained during
negotiations that all subsidiary loan agreements between the Central Bank
and participating banks, including BOR, would be subject to approval by
the Bank.  A condition of loan effectiveness would be Bank approval of at
least one subsidiary loan agreement.
4.24       Subloan Indexing (see Annex 3, Section E, for details).  The
principal of all subloans made to Project ranches would continue to be
adjusted as under Loan 698-UR and Loan 773-UTR, by a weighted index based
on ranch-gate prices of beef and wool. If changes in this index exceed
changes in the cost of living index, however, the latter would be applied
to subloans. The current indexing mechanism fully indexes the principal of
subloans on farms of more than 400 ha, and a 50% ad4ustment is applied to
loans for farms of less than 400 ha. It is estimated that these smaller
farms will receive approximately 10% of the total value of loans extended
uader the Project.  No change in this system is proposed.  Assurances were
obtained during negotiations that losses incurred by participating 'anks due
to partial-indexing only would be borne by Government and that no change in
the current indexing mechanism would be made without prior Bank approval.
4.25       Interest Rate.  The rate of 11%, established under 698-UR and
773-UR, would continue to be applied to all Project subloans, including
incremental working capital loans, contractor and seed processing loans.
Assuming an annual inflation rate of 20% and with sub-loan indexing as
described above the average real rate of interest would be approximately 93.
The partial indexing would have a differentiating effect on farms of varying
sizes, namely, for farmers above 400 ha the effective rate of interest or
adjusted principal would be 11% and for farmers below 400 ha the rate would
be about 4%.



- 1$ -
V. PRICES, PRODUCTION, MARKETS, FARMER BENEFITS
AND REVENUE GENERATION
A. Prices
5.01       Continued increases in ranchgate prices since 1969 have provided
ranchers with sufficient incentives to invest in ranch improvements. As a
result of the recent Government policy changes described earlier (para 2.14),
Project average ranchgate prices for live cattle have risen from US$0.13/kg
in May 1969 to US$0.27/kg in May 1971. During the same period, international
prices for frozen beef quarters have increased from US$460 to US$700 per ton;
October 1971 prices per m ton (quarters US$900, cooked frozen US$1,800,
special cuts US$1,500, canned beef US$1,350) continued to show increases
over these for the first five months of 1971. World beef market projections
indicate that prices will remain strong throughout the seventies. (see Annex
12 for further details). Wool prices, to the farmer, after declining
substantially, bave stabilized at US$0.42/kg. Pork prices at US$0.49/kg
are in line with EEC and US prices of US$0.61 and US$0.44/kg respectively.
B. Production and Markets
5.02       Beef.  The i.nternational demand for beef continues strong and,
according to FAD projections, it will exceed supply by 530,000 m tons by
1985. Given the substitution of pork, mutton and lamb for domestic beef
consumption, additional beef and beef products generated by the Project will
be exported. ?Measures recently adopted by the Government (para 2.14) have
provided beef processors with greater incentives to export higher quality
processed beef than to sell it on the local market.
5.03       The important US market reopened during 1970 after a year's ban on
Uruguayan beef. The United Kingdom's ban on Uruguayan carcass beef in 1969
forced the country to expand its beef export product mix and to diversify its
markets. In 1970, some 30 meat products were exported to over 50 countries.
A major meat and dairy study on further market diversification and consoli-
dation is currently being financed (partially) with proceeds from Loan 698-UR.
5.04       Mutton and Lamb.  With low wool export prices, Government has pro-
vided incentives to increase lamb production both for export and internal
consumption. Increased lamb exports should provide additional foreign ex-
change to offset lower returns from wool.
5.05       Pork.  Increased pig production generated by this Project would
be channelled mainly to the domestic market; recent studies indicate that
Uruguayans demand a lean type of fresh pork.



- 19 -
C. Processing
5.06       The slaughter capacity of existing packing houses is adequate
(Annex 1). Several newer, mediun-sized, privately-owned slaughter houses
designed to cater for the export market have begur. operations in the cattle
production areas (Map 2). Although 30 applicationis for additional plants
to be located in the production areas are pending, Government is likely to
approve only three or four over the next four years in order to prevent
possible over-capacity.
5.07       Continued Government assistance is required to maintain and improve
the meat inspection, hygiene and disease control services necessary to comply
with standards imposed by the importing countries.. Continued Government
guidance is also required in the area of meac plant modernization and expan-
sion.
D. Farmer Benefits and Revenue Gereration
5.08       Income to ranchers and financial returrs on incremental investments
have been estimated on the basis of ranch budgets (Annexes 5, 6 and 7). The
tax structure existing in May 1971 is reflected in these financial projec-
tions.   Estimated financial rates of return on ircremental investments aver-
age about 25% for beef and sheep ranches, 18% for pig producers, and 29% for
dairy beef farmers.  As under Loans 698-UR and 77--URl, assurances were ob-
tained during negotiations, that Government would maintain policies and
measures to afford efficient livestock procucers a reasonable opportunity of
obtaining a financial rate of return of at least ;5% per annum, on their new
productive livestock investments, under normal conditions of production. The
budget summary of a representative 5GG ha.  ProJecz ranch (Annex 5, Table 7)
indicates that a rancher's net income after debt service is about US$1,700
at present ana would rise to aDout US$4,300 in year 7, anc US$4,900 in year
12, and this indicates that farm incomes are adequate to encourage further
investment by farmers. Tais compares to an urban skilled worker's annual
income of about US$2,500 at present.
5.09       Public Revenue Gen.-ration.  The Projecr t5 likely to generate
a significant incremental revenue to the publac se,: .or bud-et. Using the
tax estimates made in May 1971 (Annex 1, Table 4) of US$187 per metric
:on of beef exported, the additional 49,000 m tons produced undez the Pro;--
ect at full development should generate an addit4oaal US$9., miilion annual-
ly of public revenues, equivalenc to aDout 3% of the 1971 Government budget.
VI. ECONOMIC BENEFITS AND JUTXFIK'ATION
6.01       The economic rate of return of the ProjicL is estimated to be about
31% assuming a world beef price of US$700 per m toiv (paras 5.61 and  .G2)
and Annex 12).  Should actual incremental production be valued at 10% less,



- 20 -
due either to drop in all -utDut prices or a chort fall in physical
production, the economic rate cf -eturn would %-e about 19%. This
calculation was based cn the aggregation of tn'e incremiental cash flows adjusted
for net transfer payments (taxes and subsidies) and the cost of the imported
technical services (see 4"'ex 11 for det-i!ls ef these assumptions).
6.02      At full development, after 7 years, the investments made under this
Project are likely to result in an incremental beef production of 49,000 m
tons, valued at US$34.3 million equivalent. With the new pricing policies,
and export incentives, virtuallv all of this beef should be exported, an in-
creasing portion of which ±s expected to be shipped in boneless, cooked/frozen
and special cut form. An incremental 8,000 m tons of mutton and lamb, plus
6,000 m tons of pork, are also expected to be produced from the Project,
worth US$5.6 million equivalent.
6.03      The economic rate of return specified above does not take full ac-
count of the nonquantifiable benefits cited in paragraph 3.09. The Project
would continue to introduce proven techniques of pasture improvement and
livestock production, both directly through participating producers, and
indirectly by its demonstration effect. It would also continue to emphasize
in-service training of technicians in the preparation of ranch development
plans, including detailed financial projections. Additionally, the Project
would ensure the continuation of the major tax and marketing policy reforms
introduced in 1969.
VII.  AGREMENTS REACHED AND RECOMNDATION
7.01      During negotiations, agreement was reached on the following princi-
pal points:
(a) Government would:
(i) provide the necessary funds for the annual local
currency administrative costs of the PLAN as and when
required (para 4.20); and
(ii) ensure that no change in the agreed indexing mechanism
would be made without prior Bank approval and any loss
resulting from the fact that some loans would not be
fully indexed would be borne by the Government (para 4.24);
(b) The Central Bank would:
(i) ensure that the peso equivalent of the value of
imported fertilizer and pasture seeds would be
credited to the Livestock Fund in the Central Bank
immediately upon payment of the foreign supplier by
the Bank, and used to help finance ranch development
loans made by participating banks (para 4.13);



- 21 -
(ii) ensure that withdrawal requests for development loans
made to ranchers and farmers, heifer and start-up pig
feed loans would be certified by the participating
banks, the Central Bank and the PLAN (para 4.17);
(iii) ensure that financial statements of the Project account
in participating commercial banks and those of the PLAN
would be audited by firms acceptable to the Bank. The
Project account in the Central Bank would be audited
annually by the Tribunal de Cuentas of the Borrower or
by independent auditors acceptable to the Bank (para 4.18);
(iv)  ensure that the Central Bank's Livestock Fund would
continue to be used for channelling Project funds; and
separate accounts would be kept for each category of
loans as well as technical services. Participating
banks would also establish separate accournts for
Project loan funds (para 4.19); and
(v) submit subsidiary loan agreements between Central
Bank and participating banks (including BOR) to the
Bank for approval (para 4.23).
(c) The Plan Agropecuario would:
(i) ensure that procurement of rock phosphate, fertilizer,
pasture seeds, seed production and processing
equipment and related technical service equipment
and vehicles would be carried out yv international
competitive bidding except for singie contracts whose
value would be equivalent to less than US$25,000
(para 4.16); and
(ii) ensure that any new appointment to the position of
the Technical Director or the Senior Agricultural
Economist as well as their duties, authorities and
responsibilities would be subject :o Bank approval
(para 4.21).
7.02      A condition of effectiveness of the loan is the execution of at
least one subsidiary loan agreement with a partic--pating bank (para 4.23).
7.03      With the indicated assurances, the proposed Project constitutes
a suitable basis for a Bank loan of US$25.6 million for a term. of 14 years
including a 4-year grace period. Bank finance would be made available
in two stages, US$11.2 million at this time -o finance the first year of
operations under the Project and US$14.4 million :.r. fiscal year 1973 as
soon as further commitment is required. Financing of the second stage
would be conditional upon the general creditworth&r.ess of the Borrower
at that time and on a review of the performance ur,der t'ie first stage
and an updating of this appraisal for the second.  The Borrower would be
the Government of Uruguay which would assume the K oreign exchange risk.

URUGUAY
FOURTH LIVESTOCK DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Development of the Livestock Industry
Policies and Programs
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The livestock industry is the backbone of Uruguay's economy.
Exports of beef and wool and their by-products account for approximately
85X of Uruguay's export earnings and 12% of GNP. If ancillary industries,
transport and services are taken into account the livestock sector contri-
butes 25% to 30X of GNP and employs about 19% of the total labor force.
The population of about three millions has a per capita income of about
US$600.
1.02      During tne disbursement period (1960-68) of the first two livestock
loans, a series of disincentives, inflation, discriminatory fiscal policies
and Government disregard for international hygiene requirements seriously
affected the key livestock sector.  These factors irihibited investment to
improve herd and pasture productivity and, iz. 1969, ied to a ban by the
United Kingdom and by the United States of all imLports of Uruguayan beef
products.
1.03      Since mid-1969, the Bank and the Goverment have pursued a dialogue
directed at improving the policy management and the overall investment
climate in this sector.  Improved investment incentives resulted from con-
tinually rising ranch-gate prices (Ur$33/kg liveweight in May 1969 to
Ur$67/kg in May, 1971) following internal policy reforin as well as rising
export prices for beef (US$460 per ton in May 1969 to US$700 per ton in
May 1971, and US$804 per ton in January 1972).
1.04      Mainly as a result of Uruguay livestock lo&ns 245-UR, 407-UR and
698-UR, improved pastures have increased over eO times during the past ter.
years, reaching 1,221,000 hectares.  However, nhis still only accouuts for
over 7% of the total area used for .ivestock produciLon. According to
preliminary data, the 1970 General Agricultaral CeasLs shows a 4.4% increase
in cattle population, since the 1966 census, even though beef exports have
increased 112X during the same period to 145,000 tons during 1970.



ANNEX 1
Page 2
II.  B.< CGR0UYD AND CURREN  SKTUAATTIO
Production Patterns, L:and Use and Farm Size
2.01      Uruguay has a gently undulating topography with virtually no
wasteland. Rainfall is variable, averaging 1,000 rn per annum but severe
droughts occur at about 5 year intervals. In addition, sumners are frequently
very dry and hot.
2.02      Of Uruguay's 19 million ha total area, about 16.5 million
ha are engaged for the agriculture sector and 14.9 million are used
for livestock production. This pasture land supports about 8.5 million
cattle, mainly Hereford, 600,000 dairy herd, primarily Holstein and 19.8
million head of sheep. While the cattle population increased over 4% since
the 1966 census, the sheep flock decreased 14.2% due to declining wool prices
during the period. Carrying capacity averages about one beef animal and
three sheep per ha. Since Uruguay has no more large areas of new land to
bring into use, additional livestock production must come from increased
productivity per hectare. The possibilities are great, particularly since
the larger producers (some 3,900 farmers with holdings of more than 1,000
ha (Table 1) occupy about 60% of the livestock area and manage about 60%
of Uruguay's cattle herd and sheep flock.

Production, Consumption and Exports
2.03      The increase in the international beef prices from US$460 to US$700
per ton since 1968 provided the basis for substantial farm gate rises in
live cattle prices per kg from US$0.13 to US$0.27 during the same period.
These increases ,aave Drovided the necessary incentives for improving pastures
for expanded livestock production.
2.04      The percentage of Uruguay's beef output used for domestic consump-
tion has been decreasing since the mid-1960's (Table 2). About 60% of
beef production is consumed domestically (74.5 kg/capita). Uruguay is one
of the highest beef constmers in the world (80.4 kg in Argentina and 25 kg
in Western Europe). Beef consumption has declined somewhat because of a
change in the price relationships between beef, chicken and poultry (Broiler
chicken consumption increased 150% in the past year).



ANNEX 
Page 3
2.05      With domestic wool conlsumption fairly constant at some 8,000 tons
or about 102 of production, wool with beef continues to represent the major
portion of Uruguay's exports. Its importance is diminishing and, for the
first time in 1970, export receipts from wool exports were lower than meat
and meat products.
2.06      Since the international wool market is unlikely to revive, sheep
production in Uruguay is being reoriented toward meat production (lamb and
mutton), particularly with the improved market for these products in
international markets. For the first time in a decade, in 1970 (Table 3),
Uruguay exported over 20,000 tons liveweight equivalent of iamb and mutton,
generating about US$8.5 million. Continued strong international demand and
increasing prices will provide incentives for dual purpose sheep production.
The sheep flock, which numbered 26.3 million in 1968, now stands at some
19 million. The Government has stimulated lamb production by establishing
a period between July 15 and September 30, 1971, where quality lambs,
slaughtered to satisfy domestic and external markets, will receive an
additional 10 pesos per kg on a liveweight basis.
III. POLICIES AND PR0GRA?S
3.01      During the 1960's, a series of policies penalized livestock
production, processing and exports. These policies were.
(a) Heavy export taxes on beef and wool;
(b) Sales taxes on all cattle and wool sold;
(c) Irregular fertilizer subsidies;
(d) An inefficient government dominated meat oacking industry;
(e) A deteriorating meat inspection and hygieae control
service; and
(f) Heavy import duties on agricultural machinery.
3.02      The positive technical achievements in pasture improvement and
increased carrying capacity under Loans 245-UR and 407-UR were offset by
these adverse Government policies. Without their modification, further
financial support for investments in primary p:oduc_ioa would have been
largely meaningless. Moreover, livestock devel-opmeait objectives needed to
move beyond the production phases and inciade :he processing and marketing
industry. Remedial Government action was required before the Bank could
continue financial support of the livestock development program.
3.03      Since mid-1969, the Bank and Government pursued a dialogue directed
at improving policy management and the overalI inves.tment climate in the
Livestock sector. The results of these policy changes outlined in quantitative
terms in Tables 4 and 5, introduced through Loa-.n 69r,-nR, exceeded expectations
-- both for the rancher and for the economy. Beef exports are increasing,
as are also the local production and consumption of such substitutes as pork,
mutton and chicken; improvements have been made ir the structure and staffing
of the meat inspection services; ana private meat packinghouses have invested
in plant modernization, including the upgrading of vital hygiene standards.



ANNEX 1
Page  L
3.04      The following steps were aimed mainly at improving producers'
investment incentives:
(a)  internal consumer prices for beef were raised 15% on
the average, helping to reduce domestic consumption
and made additional beef available for export
(para 2.04);
(b)  beef export taxes were changed from an ad valorem tax of
15% to a flat rate of US$80 per ton of beef exported, an
average equivalent to 10% ad valorem, to induce meat packers
to export higher quality, more processed cuts of beef;
(c)  wool export taxes were reduced in stages from 29% to 5%
to maintain wool exports;
(d)  import duties and surcharges on farm machinery were
removed; and
(e)  ranch-gate price controls on beef cattle were abolished.
The relation between rising international prices of beef and consumer prices
are readjusted regularly. Accordingly, beef price increases to the consumer
have been 40% since 1969, which has encouraged consumption of other meats
such as mutton, pork and poultry.
IV. PROCESSING AND MARKETING OF
LIVESTOCK PRODUCTS
4.01      A major political effort has been made since 1969 to restructure
the beef processing industry. Issues still remaining are both political and
technical, and if squarely met with sound technical and marketing advice,
continued improvements are likely over the next few years. The importance
of the 4 large old plants in the city has diminished and some 13 smaller
and more efficient privately owned plants, outside of Montevideo, now account
for a major portion of Uruguay's beef exports. These smaller plants have
invested over US$8 million in installing modern processing capacity and
improving facilities to meet hygiene standards. Part of this investment
plus additional funds for further plant modernization is to be supported by
the Inter-American Development Bank (IDB) loan of US$13.7 million (Table 6).
4.02      Processing costs in Uruguay were US$107 per ton in May 1969, as
compared to US$30 in Argentina. Policy changes (para 3.02 and 3.04) initiated
by the Government since 1969 have begun to show beneficial effects and in
May 1971, the beef processing costs were US$83 per ton.



ANNEX 1
Page 5
Structure and Status of the Processing Industry
4.03      The structure of Uruguay's meat industry has changed significantly
in the past decade. The international meat packing compapies no longer
operate in Uruguay. Of the old and largely inefficient plants, three are
located in Montevideo. One is owned and operated by Government and the
others by a worker's cooperative. The fourth is located on Uruguay River
and has dock facilities. The remaining 13 plants located outside Montevideo
are medium-sized and export oriented.
4.04      Most of the commercial livestock slaughter excluding the non-
inspected slaughterhouses and farm slaughters, is handled by 18 meat plants -
11 of these 18 plants are situated within 65 km of Montevideo and account
for most of the export meat and meat products. Seven medium-sized plants
contribute 70% of the country's beef exports. The slaughter capacity is
considerably in excess of current or medium term rec,uirements. However, the
effective chilling and freezing capacity, is only slightly above the current
slaughter capacity.
4.05      The operating and sanitary conditions of the meat plants varied
widely in 19690from barely acceptable to totally unacceptable. The lack
of a satisfactory meat inspection service and enforcible regulations led to
the United Kingdom and the United States placing an embargo on all imports
of meat and meat products from Uruguay. As a result, the Government initiated
action to establish an adequate and recognized meat inspection service and
a plan of action to modernize the meat packing industry.
4.06      As a result of Government sponsored action, the modernization of
the meat industry began slowly during early 1970. Duriag 1969 and 1970, over
US$8 million was put primarily into the private sector to improve facilities.
4.07      All of Uruguay's meat plants produce for export, chilled and
frozen beef carcasses, and edible offals. All but one export special cuts
and boneless beef. Three plants have facilities for producing canned beef
and extract.  Three other plants plan on adding canning facilities to their
operation within the next five years. Most plan to add cooked frozen pro-
cessing during the next five years also.  Tallow, blood meal and bonemeal
are produced for export by the larger plants and one or twq smaller ones.
4.08      During the past 18 months, significant improvement was made in
the sanitary conditions of the newer medium-sized plants. Effective meat
inspection service has resulted in marked changes in operating procedures.
More efficient processing facilities, flow patterns and processes have been
introduced primarily in the private sector. On the rail dressirng, continuous
boning systems and new special cut techniques have bean successfully
introduced. Additional chill and freezer storage is urgently needed.
4.09      Labor productivity differs greatly from the large older plants
where excess employment exists to the smaller new plants where the ratio of
production workers to office staff is about 6 to 1. Smaller private owned
plants, operating away from Montevideo do not suffer to the same extent from
strikes and labor difficulties.



ANNEX 1
Page 6
4.10      As a result of the dialogue between the IDB and the Government an
organization, Unidad Profrigos, under the Ministry of Industry and Commerce
has been established by Government decree. This orfice has the responsibility
for coordination between agencies, the reorganization and modernization of
the meat processing industry and for implementation of the IDB loan. A
commission, consisting of representatives from the Central Bank, the
Ministry of Agriculture and Livestock and the Profrigos Unit, is responsible
for the necessary coordination between these agencies to assure that hygiene
standards, sanitary engineering, economic considerations, industry planning
and construction standards meet international requirements and the goals of
Uruguay. The Profrigos Unlit makes technical and economic evaluations of
the meat processing industry requests for building new facilities, modernizing
existing plants and increasing capacities. Emphasis is placed on sanitary
engineering, efficient process flow, product mix, facilities design, adequacy
of storage and services and location.
4.11      Recent ?o±icy Changes.  In an effort to solve industrial problems,
Government took a series of important policy steps between M4y and November
1969. In this period, Government:
(a) substituted a cash payment for that portion of the
Frigounl's worker's wage received in beef;
(b) abolished Frigonal's monopoly of cattle slaughter 8nd
beef supply to the Montevideo market. All meat packing
plants are now able to supply the domestic market under
a flexible quota system;
(c) designated funds in the 1970 budget that permitted the
transfer or retirement of 450 of Frigonal's administrative
personnel;
(d) estab'.ished a 3Z sales tax on all meat sold by the
privately and cooperatively owned packing plants in
the dcmestic ma-.ket.  The proceeds of this tax, to
be held in a special fund, are to be used for
(1) restructuring the industry, (2) improving the
marketing of beef, and (3) compensating blue collar
workers laid off; and
(e) abolished all import duties and surcharges on the
equipment imported for the modernization program of
the packing indu.3try. The average level of import
duties, sales tax, port charges, and the like on such
equipment varied from 50Z to 100% of the CIF values.
4.12      Substantial action and investment, however, is still needed to
prepare the meat packing industry for the rapidly changing international
demand pattern emerging for the 1970's. Determinations must be made by
Government as to:



ANNEX 1
Page 7
(a) basic structure and ownership of the industry. Currently,
the packing industry, in terms of slaughter capacity, is
divided roughly into thirds: private ownership, public
ownership and worker's cooperatives;
(b) slaughtering capacity, freezing and cold storage
facilities and the location of new processing plants; and
(c) patterns for investment to upgrade hygienic standards and
to modernize slaughterhouse facilities.
Modernizing the Processing Industry
4.13      There is general recognition throughout the industry that
facilities and equipment must be upgraded to permit more efficient slaughter-
ing and processing operations and that hygienic facilities must be improved.
The industry is realistic in recognizing the urgent need to set up an
approved inspection system and to ensure acceptance of its products in the
export market.
4.14      Since 1969, the private sector has invested over US$8 million to
upgrade existing slaughtering and processing facilities to meet international
sanitary and hygiene requirements, for the production of value added products
and to improve operating efficiency. Additional investments are required to
replace the large old plants at EFCSA, Frigonal and Fray Bentos, conversion
of existing facilities for processing canned meats, cooked frozen, boneless
cuts, vacuum packaged and frozen meats, and increasing chilling and freezing
storage and blast freezing capabilities. With the availability of larger
supplies of lamb and pigs being projected, additional slaughtering and
processing facilities will be required. The limiting factor in meat pro-
duction for expqrt is the refrigerated holding capacity. An expansion in
export volume can be achieved only by upgrading and expanding the processing
and cooler freezer facilities.
Meat Inspection and Hygiene Control
4.15      Government has initiated a number of measures to improve its
meat inspection and hygiene control services. Major changes are:
(a) Payment of meat inspectors by Government;
(b) Rotation and training of meat inspectors;
(c) Training of three engineers in facilities design
for hygiene control;
(d) Reorganization of the administration structure of
the meat inspection service;



ANNEX I
?age S
(e)  Start of construction on a national system of
truck and railcar washing, and disinfectant
points to control possible spread of foot and
mouth disease; and
(f)  Large increase in the number of meat inspection
personnei and better in-service training.
4.16      These measures are beginning to have results.  The United States
have cleared one plant and Western Germany has approved several plants.
Uruguay is exporting to over 40 countries presently.
4.17      Since the Government started reorganizing the meat inspection
service, by issuing a decree (May 14, 1969) that established a basis for an
independent, well directed, well paid, full time Government service, the
above listed changes have resulted in many improvements within the plants
and in the quality of the beef being exported.
Marketing
4.18      Meat plant offcials depend almost entirely on private sources
for market information and none of the private plants obtainl market inform-
ation from the GovernmenL.  There is a serious deficiency in curnt foreign
market information available.  Such an important area should be given much
more consideration and attention by both the meat plants and the Government.
In any pragram to maintain and increase export outlets, market information
is critical; therefore, steps hae been initiated to establish a Meat Board,
which will have broad responsibilities in marketing.
4.19      The Government has recognized the urgent need for a comprehensive
international marketing study to provide the meat processing industry, the
producer and the Government with market information for future planning as
well as for the 44av to Aav functionIng of this key industry.  A major
studv of TTruquav' s external meat and dairy markets is being conducted
by an international consulting firm. The study will provide projections on
demand for meat and meat products by product mix, promotional requirements,
product presentation, information on factors affecting demand changes,
Government policies on meat imports and hygiene standards and changing patterns
of consumption. The study will' review other exporting countries policies
and projected exports in their affect on Uruguay's future exports.
4.20      Although data is skimpy on what the future international beef
product mix wiLl be, Uruguay's beef exports are shifting from chilled and
frozen carcass beef to frozen special cuts, boneless products, cooked
frozen, chilled vacuum packaged meat and other value added products. Addi-
tional beef produced under the ?lan target have b!en included in thie proj-
ected beef export product miix for the next four years.
