Technical Services
A. Consultant and Technical Services
1.        The important technical services provided under the Bank supported
program would be continued (Table 1). In addition to the Plan Agropecuario
extension services, consultant and technical services would be provided to
support Government's effort to:
(a)  improve pasture research related to fertilizer
and grazing management trials, assist in Estenzuela's
pasture seed certification and processing program; and
(b) reorganize the meat and dairy processing industry
in Uruguay, including:
(1) modernization, expansion and improvement of
meat and dairy processing plants; and
(ii) improvement of the meat processing industry
regulation and of the meat inspection and marketing
system.
Related goods and equipment to carry out the above technical services would
also be supported under the Project.
B. Plan Agropecuario
Back round
2.        The Plan Agropecuario (PLAN) was established in 1959 to provide
technical services to participating Project ranchers. It helps farmers
prepare suitable ranch plans, recommends subloans to the Bank of the Republic
(BOR) and provides technical advice during the disbursement period. The
PLAN also provides some extension services and supports applied research
in pasture development and pasture seed production.
Performance
3.        Since 1959 the PLAN' has successfully demonstrated and promoted
the advantages of establishing improved pasture. It has maintained a



ANNEX 2
Page 2
constant lending volume during the very difficult period from 1965 to mid-
1969 when loan size was reduced by factors mainly beyond the control of
the PLAN. Overall, the PLAN has done its work well. It has a competent
staff and, with organizational changes now in progress, its performance
should improve further.
Management
4.        The PLAN is d-.rected by an Honorary Comission of seven members:
a Chairman, four delegares from the various farmer organizations, one from
the Ministry of Agriculture and Livestock and one from the BOR (Figure 1,
Annex 2).
Technical Staff
5.        The Technical Staff comprises a Technical Director, about 60
technicians and 11 office staff. The three Technical Directors that have
served since commencement of the PLAN have been internationally recruited.
The present Technical Director was a New Zealand professor in Farm
Management.
6.        The techniciars, all university graduates, have a good reputation
for technical competence. They are recruited following a rigorous entry,
written and oral, examination which takes three days. They are each
equipped with a vehicle and live in rural centers for better contact with
the ranchers.
C. Duties, Responsibilities and Authorities of the Technical
Director <.nd the Senior A&ricultural Economist
Technical Director
7.        The Technical Director shall be responsible to the Honorary
Commission and have the technical responsibility for implementation and
execution of the Fourth Livestock Development Project. He shall have the
following specific duties and powers:
(a)  advising the Honorary Commission on major policy decisions
in respect to the Project;
(b)  executing the Project in accordance with policies and
procedures agreed to by thA Honorary Commission;
(c)  cooperating with the management of participating banks
towards accomplishing Project objectives;
(d)  training PLAN staff as necessary for fulfillment of
Project objectives;



ANNEX 2
Page 3
(e)  approving or disapproving all ranch/farm development
plans prepared and appraised under the lending program;
(f) following up, supervising and giving technical assistance
as necessary to ensure success of each farm development
plan financed under the Project;
(g) advising participating banks through the Honorary Commission
to suspend or premature loans when he finds that the
farm development plan for which the loan has been granted
is not being carried out in accordance with the terms of
the loan agreement. He shall inform the participating
bank, through the Honorary Commission, if and when the
situation has been satisfactorily rectified;
(h) establishing and maintaining farm records for sample farms
as needed for Project evaluation;
Ci)  making recommendations to the Honorary Commission with
respect to:
i)  remuneration for PLAN staff and other compensation
necessary to attract the most qualified personnel
available; and
(ii) the recruitment, suspension or removal of PLAN staff;
Cj) preparation of quarterly and annual progress reports for
submission to the Honorary Commission for transmittal to the
Rank: and
(k) guidance to agencies, principally the Estenzuela Agricultural
Research Center, concerned with the preparation and execution
of Project-oriented pasture/beef research work and an improved
seed production and processing scheme.
Senior Agricultural Economist
8.        The Senior Agricultural Economist as a technical officer of the
Commission, shall be responsible directly to the Commission and the Executive
Technical Director in the execution of the following duties:
(a) Design, organize and execute a system of careful on-farm
record keeping to assess the technical and ecnnomic impact
of PLAN supported investments;
(b)  Assess the economics of different types of on-farm fodder and
hay conservation under different price and ecological conditions
in Uruguay;



ANNEX 2
Page 4
(c) Design, organize and maintain careful indexes of the on-farm
prices of major inputs (fertilizer, seed, machines, etc.) and
outputs (cattle, sheep, wool, crops) used by PLAN borrowers;
(d)  Assess periodically the economics (using the internal rate of
return) of different systems of pasture improvement and
ranch management in the various ecological zones of Uruguay.
He will work d:;rectl'y under the Technical Director in this
matter; and
(e) Work closely wi.th the economists of the Estanzuela research
station in assessing the costs and benefits of their many
practical and successful on-farm grass/legume fertilizer
trials carried out largely on commerclal farms throughout
Uruguay.
D. Research Prolects and Seed Certification
Research
9.        Eight specific research sub-projects are being carried out at
Estanzuela Research Station In support of Plan Agropecuario Project activities.
Project funds (US$200,000) would be used in support of these activities to
purchase necessary equiment and machines.
10.       The main research areas include:
(a) studies on Agroclimatology for different regions and
hydrological characteristics of main soil types;
(b) soil fertility studies covering the main regions with
special emphasis on requirements for pasture;
(c) studies on crop and pasture rotations in rice growing
areas and on soils with erosion problems;
(d) evaluation of production possible from introduced
grasses and legumes on a regional basis under different
systems of management;
(e)  studies on pasture utilization and management including
the use of annual seasonal pastures;
(f) studies on seed production including fertilizer require-
ments and methods of establishment and management for
various grasses and legumes;



ANNEX 2
Page 5
(g)  research aimed at developing high producing beef
farming systems on Improved pasture, with special
emphasis on stock management techniques;
(h)  studies on genetic Improvement of beef cattle by
performance and progeny testing and evaluation of main
beef breeds and crosses; and
(i)  studies on sheep production and management with particular
emphasis on reproduction.
Seed Certification
11.       The goal of this Program is the supply to farmers of adequate
volumes of seeds of high yielding varieties that have been previously eva-
luated by the Centro and have been multiplied, processed and distributed
under technical control. The certification system is adapted to the
standards established by specialized international organizations. The last
step in this system is the production of certified seed which is carried
out by specialized producers generally grouped in cooperatives strategically
distributed In the different regions of the country. This program is being
developed satisfactorily and is highly supported by farmers and credit
organizations. Progress in the respective research projects has permitted
the number of crop and forage plant varietles accepted for certification
to increase gradually. The Seed Certification Program supplies the farmers
with permanent technical assistance.

FOURTH LIVESTOCK DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Credit, Services and Facilities
A. Background
1.        Two types of credit institutions lend to the agricultural subsec-
tor: official and private. Official banks include the monolithic Bank of
the Republic, with 52% of the total banking rural systems loan portfolio;
the National Mortgage Bank; the State Savings Bank; and the State Insurance
Bank 1/. There are also 32 private banks -- down from 48 in 1969.
2.        Inflation 2/, overstaffing and restrictive credit po'licies have
put the banking system under considerable pressure in recent years. Thus,
the private banking system is now undergoing a severe restructuring and
consolidation. Since early 1970, 16 commercial banks, including the
second largest Uruguayan-owned bank, Banco Mercantil, have declared bank-
ruptcy and a number of other private banks are, in muid-1971, considering
merger to stave off insolvency. Parliament has further added to the banking
uncertainty by passing, in preliminary form, legislation that would nation-
alize the banking system. Committees of Parliament were organized in mid-1971
to study various measures of resolving the banking crises, measures which
did not exclude nationalization. While 32 private commercial banks are
clearly excessive for a population of less than 3 million and a GDP of
US$1.7 billion per annum, the shakeout of these 32 co six or seven viable
commercial banks is likely to take some time and result in considerable
uncertainty in the interim.
3.        This annex describes the changing level o:- agricultural credit,
the Plan Agropecuario program, the results of index-ing Project subloans
after three years and the possible organization of a second lending channel,
additional to the Bank of the Republic (BOR).
1/   Credit is also provided by a parallel crediz market, though the activi-
ties of this sector have been restricted to some extent since mid-1971.
2/   To overcome the effects of inflation and a progressive increase in over-
heads, the banks have generally adopted the following policies: (a)
increased loan interest to 4% per month; (b) reduced loan terms to 6
months; (c) switched lending avenues, where possible, to those of little
risk, quick return and high profitability (speculative ventures) and in
the case of the BOR indexes had to be applied to loans7 made under Plan
Agropecuario (from January 1969).



ANNEX 3
Page 2
B. Central Bank and Monetary Policy
4.        The Central Bank (CB) was established in 1967 when BOR relinquished
its central banking functions, particularly management of the money supply.
It now has legal control over the supply of money and credit and is empow-
ered to invoke normal stabilization measures such as (a) discount rate con-
trol; (b) changes in the legal reserve ratio requirements of the banks;
(c) establishment of credit ceilings on portfolio structure; and (d) interest
rates.
Organization
5.        The policy of the Central Bank is set by a five-member Board of
Directors, all appointed by the Government. The Department of Credit has
two divisions dealing with credit operations with commercial banks: the
Discount Sector and the Plarn Agropecuario Fund (Figure 1). The Discount
Sector is important in the operation of discount lines both with Government
and banks, and is responsible for influencing the level of credit, the rates
of discount, and rates of interest on deposits and loans. The Plan
Agropecuario division, formed in early 1971, deals solely with Project
lending arrangements.
6.        In early 1968, severe credit restrictions were imposed on the
commercial banks, both on their local currency and foreign currency opera-
tions. In addition to ceilings on the portfolio structure, high reserve
requirements were also established, 40% on demand deposits, 20% on time
deposits and 16% on foreign currency deposits 1/. This policy, together with
the measures taken by the commercial banks (footnote 2, para 2), resulted
in a period of relative monetary stability but created significant short-
term credit problems in the livestock industry, which was nearly cut off
from all short-term funds for 12 months in 1968/69.  Loans to agriculture
during this period declined over 30% when adjusted for inflation. This
restriction of funds has now been lifted and the short-term credit needs of
the livestock sector are being met partially by BOR and to some extent by
commercial banks.
7.        In 1970, the ceilings on the portfolio structure of commercial
banks were removed except that the CB still requires commercial banks
to use their time deposits (more than 12 months) mainly (75%) for development
purposes, such as loans to livestock, agriculture and industrial activities.
0 these loans, at least 50% have to be made for terms of not less than 12
months.

8.        The CB has established limits on interest rates paid out by
commercial and official banks on fixed-term deposits (30 days or more)
as follows:
More than
Amount Ur$                3 Months    6 Months       12 Months
50,000 to 250,000                  13%          14%           17%
250,000 to 1,000,000               14%          15%           18%
1,000,000 to 5,000,000             15%          16%          19%
More than 5,000,000                16%          17%           20%
However, on actual lending operations, the CB has established that the
deposit banks (official or commercial) shall not charge more than 24% interest
per annum, including all types of charges, except a 6% once-and-for-all tax
on the original loan charged to the borrower. Despite these controls, com-
mercial banks have added various commissions, surcharges and compensating
balance requirements, which have increased the effective interest rate from
24% to over 40%.
Indexing
9.        The CB has in two cases permitted indexing of loans.  The Mortgage
Bank (MB) is allowed to issue adjustable mortgage bonds. Also, medium- and
long-term loans to livestock producers borrowing funds partially provided
by Rank are adjusted according to an index based on the farm-gate price of
wool and beef. Section E provides details on the latter system.
Discount Operations
10.       In addition to economic regulation activities, the.C  has an
important role in the financing of productive activities through its discount
operations. From 1969, more than Ur$ 8,500 million (US$34 million) have been
channeled to specified activities through the commercial banks by means of
the discount lines granted by the CB. Comparing outstanding lines of dis-
count between 1969 and 1970, the CB had the following figures:

C. Official Banks
Bank of the Republic
11.       The BOR has been for many years the country's largest bank.  It
has been banker to the Government and to the rural, industrial and civil
service sectors. Its operations are especially important in agriculture,
exceeding slightly in portfolio all other commercial banks combined, as the
Table below indicates.

12.       Organization.  One of the oldest banks in the 2ountry, BOR is an
integrated organization wlhose policy is determined by a Board of Directors,
appointed by the Government (rigure I for nrganizational Structure).
BOR is well distributed throughout the country, withl 71 rural branches, two
rural agencies, six city agencies and a main office in Montevideo.
13.       BOR's Credit and External Business Departments are the most
impnortant in the lending activities. The Credit Department itself has six
Divisions.  In 1971, the group dealing with Plan Agropecuarlo lending was
raised to division level within the Credit Department and p'aced under a
full-time single manager.  This has improved BOR's PLending activities.
i4.       Financial Operations.  A large part of BOR assets and liabilities
arc ei ,her Government or Government-related (see tPables 7 to 3
f<,r details).  Until 1967, when the Cenrtral Bank was established, all official
ante semiofficial accounts were kept in the BOR.  Most of these accounts still
remain.  Nevertheless, its private sector lending activities are important,
especially in agriculture.
15.       Plan iAropecuarLo.  Up to mid-1971, the BOR was the only partici-
pating bank for the relending of Rank loan funds to Plan Agropecuario
ranchers. BOR, in addition, has been the relending agency for several
USAID loans for cereal growing, machinery anU for genera, purpose agriculture.
It is also "he channel for a number of IDB loans for industrial clevelopment.

17.       From 1966 to 1970, there was a six-fold increase in rural lending
through BOR, but in real terms, this increase was only 80% because the
value of the peso changed in 1968 when a new devaluation took place. Most
of the loans granted by BOR to the rural sector were for working capital

18.       From the above, the decrease in the agricultural short-term
lending by the BOR is clearly evident in 1967 and 1968.
19.       Plan Lending.  All individual loans under the TILAN have been
made through the BOR. These loans are controlled by the Plan Agropecuario
Division, the Manager of which is responsible for practice and policy
regulations regarding the processing of all plans and applications. Until
1967, all PTAN loans were made by the central office division directly.
However, for the last three years, the bulk of the lending decisions has



ANNEX 3
Page 6
been made by branch managers after discussion with the local .PLAN technician,
which has speeded up the process considerably. Loan decisions depend upon
both the technical program of development drawn up by PLAN technicians and
on the borrower's credit-worthiness and tax payment status.
20.       Recent Legal Changes.  Under Law 12394, Art. 11 (1957), the proceeds
of the first two Bank loans were channeled exclusively through the Plan
Agropecuario Fund in the BOR. Rancher subloan amortization was paid by the
BOR into Uruguay's General Revenue to service the Bank loans. Government
absorbed both the bad debt and the exchange risks (Law 12394, Article 14)
because the Government-owned BOR was both a first floor bank and a central
bank. However, with the creation of the CB, the arrangements were changed
and now the CB is responsible for servicing the Bank loan and also for
carrying the exchange risks; the bad debt risk is taken care of by BOR.
In the event that any commercial bank becomes a lending channel for Rank
loans, similar contractual arrangements would be made, under which bad debt
risks would likewise be borne.
21.       Accounting and Auditing.  The systems followed by BOR for the
handling, storage and retrieval of accounting data are being improved by the
use of electronic processing equipment. However, credit statistics are still
handled slowly, especially in regard to the proper linkage of the operations
in the branches and their recording, processing and interpretation at the
central office. Internal auditing is done by the General Inspection Depart-
ment of BOR.
Banco Nacional Hipotecario (State Mortgage Bank - MB)
22.      The MB is a Government bank that had been operating mainly as an
urban mortgage institution but it also handles certain types of loans for
the rural sector. Before 1948, the MB had two types of operations: supplying
regular mortgage credit to assist private owners to sell or buy land and
nouses under a mortgage security system, and assisting small farmers to
obtain land under the agrarian reform and colonization laws. In 1948, and
based on a section of the MB, Instituto Nacional de Colonization (INC) was
created. It took the responsibility of lending money for redistribution of
land. Recent MB operations in the rural sector have gone from 8% of its
1961 portfolio to 17% in 1970 1/.
instituto Nacional de Colonizacion (INC)
23.       The INC is legally able to serve as intermediary on all land
transactions as well as to finance land sales. It was established as an
entity under the MB, with distinct legal and economic personality; its
capital was formed by all land holdings of the Section of Rural Development
and Colonization of the MB as well as the funds from a 'ont4 Issue of
Ur$ 20 million in 1948.
tt In 1970, the MB accumulated large deposits from an earmarked tax.
These funds were to be lent for housing construction which has lagged
behind schedule.

D. Commercial Banks
24.       Twenty-eight Uruguayan and four foreign banks, plus an unknown
number of exchange companies that perform specific exchange activities,
make up the private banking system (Table 4).  Among the Uruguayan
banks, only two remain of reasonable size: the Banco Commercial and the
Banco La Caja Obrera. Of the foreign banks, only the Bank of London and
South America and the First National Bank are of importance. Both of these
latter are considering an increase in the number of their rural offices.
Role of Commercial Banks in Agricultural Credit
25.       The volume of deposits in BOR and the comm.ercial banks amour.tcu
to more than Ur$ 78,000 m in 1971 (US$3'2 million), only 5.4,. of which was
kept in BOR; the rest went into the 32 commercial baniks. From the total
outstandlng balances on discounts granted bv Ce of [.rS 37,540 million, onlv
AS were granted to the 32 commercial banks. During the 1970 fiscal year,
commercial banks received a total TTrS 3,462 million (USS13.S million) from
CB discounts (Table 5). Most of the discounts were handled by the
official banks (Table 6).
26.       On the other hand, the total volume of credit provided by officia1
and private banks during 1970 was Ur$ 248.669 million, of which only 19% was
handled by commercial banks. The rural sector got only 9.4%, participated
in equally by BOR and commercial banks.
Additional Lending Channels
27.       As the Bank-supported lending program is covering over 4,500
ranchers, additional lending channels will be required if the full develop-
ment loan demand is to be adequately met. In December 1970, the CB notified
the banking system that all banks were considered eligible as relending
agencies for the indexed Plan loans. As a result, three showed interest,
presenting at the same time two caveats concerning participation in the
program: (a) the low interest spread, and (b) the difficulty the commercial
banks have had in obtaining adequate mortgage security.
28.       To encourage participation, the Government agreed to:  (a) provide
a more suitable combination of interest spread and percentage of discount
(3.5% on 902 reimbursement); (b) pass legislation easing the complications
of mortgage registering as security; (c) ensure that 'PLkV technicians under-
take full farm budgeting procedures where necessary; and (d) ensure provision
of training courses for personnel in the private banks in medium- and long-
term lending activities in livestock.



ANEX 3
Page 8
E. Indexing
29.       Under the Fourth Project, subloans to farmers will be on the same
basis as for Loans 698-UR and 773-UR. All borrowers with 400 ha or more
will be subject to full indexing, and those with less than 400 ha will have
their loans adjusted by only 50%. Although loans for farms under 400 ha
are expected to be numerous, the major portion of funds lent will be subject
to full monetary correction.
30.       The indexing system devised by Government and now accepted by the
BOR and participating farmers was carefully worked out by the Plan with the
CB and discussed at great length during the Third Project appraisal in
June 1969. It appears that all the parties accept the system -- now legally
in force. The confidence that the farmers have in the Government's ability
to ensure them a satisfactory price for beef and wool and maintain that
price at acceptable levels in the light of changes in economic conditions
over the next decade will likely be directly related to the confidence they
will have in the system of indexing loans.
Choice of Index
31.       While acknowledging that indexing is a second-best solution, it
is nevertheless important to select the type of index that best maintains
rancher and lender confidence. Thus the problem is not the acceptability
of indexing in principle, but the acceptability to the lenders and borrowers
concerned in this particular Project of the index selected. For this rea-
son, it would be unrealistic and fruitless to search for an ideal index
that would cover all types of development lending in Uruguay. Though an
arbitrary one could be devised by Government to apply throughout the economy
on all loans, the result would probably be that borrowers and lenders in
areas out of line with the average would stop using it and rely on some other
method of compensating lenders for inflation. They might even regress to
a point of not entering into financial contracts at all.
Indexing Subloans to Beef and Wool Prices
32.       To encourage the vital livestock industry and the livestock devel-
opment program supported by this Project, Government is now using an index
on subloans, made under Plan auspices, which is based on the ranch-gate price
of beef and wool. The system, which has been accepted by over 4,000 ranchers
since 3anuary 1969, uses as a base the value of 41.6 kg of beef and 5.2 kg
of wool, calculated as the average during the period of one year between
November 1 and October 31. These index components are based on the national
average of beef and wool per ha.
33.       The CB is charged with calculating the adjustments for the period
November I to October 31 each year. It assembles data from the national
stockyard, from the national meat packing plants, from the chamber of com-
merce or from such other agencies as it may consider appropriate. Before



ANNEX 3
Page 9
March 31 of each year, the CB forwards to the BOR and any coimnercial partic-
ipating bank the adjustment factor necessary for each loan in the Project.
A translation of the legal document that borrowers now sign to obtain an
indexed loan is presented as Appendix 1 to t;his annex. In February 1970,
loans were indexed 8.7% and, in February 1971, a furtner 18.6% 1/.
1/ As world beef prices have increased by miore chan 50% between 1969 anti
1971 (US$460 to US$700 per ton), Government ?roposed in late 1970 that
the cost of living index be used as an upper limit whenever the meat
and wool index rise exceeds the cost of livi:ng rise. This was accepzed
by the Bank and this revised index is now effective.
February 11, 1972

CONTRACTUAL CLAUSES
The loan which this declaration is referred to will be adjustable according
to the following clauses:
a)   The Base Index - which will be equivalent to 100 - for determining
the adjustmnt factor, will be calculated or. the average value of
41.6 (forty-one kilogram 600 grams) of beef (fattened steer on the
hoof) and of 5.2 (five kilograms 200 grams) of wool (fleeces from
selected breeds of superior quality and above) during the period
of ome year betveen Noeer 1,          and October 31, _.
b) The adjustment will be made by taking as base the index of positive
increase in value of 41.6 kilogram of beef (fattened steer on the
hoof) and of 5.2 kilogram of wool (fleeces from selected breeds of
superior quality and above) provided by the National Stockyard in
6ccordaace with data from the National Meat Packing Plant and the
Qiamber of Comoerce, or obtained from such agencies as nay substitute
for them in the judgent of the Central Bank of Uruguay.
c) The adjustment index will be forwarded to the Bank by the Central
Bank of Uruguay before March 31 of each year.
d)   In determining the adjustment index the Central. Bank of Uruguay
will take the average prices fetched by the products indicated
in subclause b), for the year between November 1 and October 31
of the following year.
e)   Whem the value of the adjustment index is equaL to or less than
100, no revaluation of the loans will be made for that year.
f)   If, on April 30 of each year, the Bank does not have ava'lable
tho information needed to apply the corresponcAng adjustment
index, the payment made by the debtor will be accepted as a
paymut on account.
8)   The adjustment shall be applied, in accordance with the base
index established in subclause a) above, to the corresponding
fmortization installment, in such a manner that the debtor shall
repay on each occasion the amortization instaLlment due, together
with the aiount of the reevaluation in accordance with the
adjustment factor applying at the time calculated on the base
index.
h)   During the period between November 1 and April 20 of the
following year, no advanced payments shall be collected.



ANNEX 3
Appendiz 1
Page 4
Nevertheless, if the debtor should wish to make a deposit,
, the Bank shall receive the deposit
but it shall be charged to amrtization of the debt after the
corresponding revaluation has been made, the foregoing being
entirely without prejudice to the provisions of clause J) below.
i) If it is desired to make amortization payments prior to November 1
no adjustment shall be collected in respect of these. For
the following years, if amortitation payments are made prior to
Novmber t, they shall be subject to the adjustment in respect
of the t ediately preceding April 30.
j)   In the case of payments on account, priority should be given
first to the payment of the adjustment, then to the payment of
interest and finally to amortisation payments. Interest on the
loam, at the rate of % yearly, shall be calculated on the total
capital sum or outstanding balances plus the adjustments on
these balances.
k) A-Drtimtion payments that are more than six months overdue,
or any such payments for which the Bank may have allowed more
than six months for payment, shall be subject to the adjustment
index In respect of the period between the following November 1
and October 31.
1)   Without prejudice to the provisions of the foregoing clauses,
the Bank shall determine the percentage increase in the
revaluation index to be applied each year in relation to the
area that the debtor may for any purpose be farming.
a)   The revaluation index to be applied each year, taking the
average prices fetched by the products indicated in clause b),
shall be limited by the increase in the cost of living - price
index for the same twelve-month base period used for the beef
and wool index according to data from the General Direction of
Statistics of the Ministry of Economics.

LeadizA Policies and Procedures
A. Participating Banks
1.        Under the Project, eligible participating banks would lend to
ranchers and borrowrs based on the criteria outlined below:
(a)  Ugtbility - Participating banks would determine the finan-
cial creditworthiness of borrowers as the basis for lending.
They would not alter the ranch or farm plan as approved by
the PLAN.
(b)  Terms and Conditions -
(i)  Development loans to ranchers and farmers would be
made for 80X of the investment costs stated in the
approved ranch plan. Working capital loans (heifer
retention and start-up pig feed costs) would be made
for 100X of the approved amounts, such amounts being
established solely from the farm budget approved by
the PLAN. Loans to contractor's would be made for
7OS of the congcactor's PLAN approved investments.
(ii) The indexing mechanism used under Loan 698-UR which
was established by law in January 1970 would be
applied to the principal of all subloans made under
the Project (see Annex 3 for details).
(iii) The term of development loans to ranchers and borrowers
would be recommended to participating banks by the PLAN
on the basis of the farm budget and zhe ability of the
borrower to service his debt from additional income
generated from the Project supported investments. The
minimum term would be seven years and maximum 10 years
with a maximum grace period of 2 and 4 years respectively.
Contractor loans would be made for a minimum of five and
maximum of seven years with a grace period of one year.
The term of the working capital loans would be based on
the budget considerations arising out of the cash analysis
of the farm budget with a minimum and maximum term of
3 to 7 years respectively and including a minimum and
maximum grace period of 1 to 3 years.
(iv)  The interest rate would be 11% on al. loans, inclusive
of all charges, fees and commissions. Any changes in
this rate would be subject to Bank approval.
(c) Approvals - All ranch, farm and contractor proposed invest-
ment plans would be approved by the PLAN and any proposed

B1k approval.
(d)  Disbursments sd  1T    - Participating banks would keep

have such accounts audited by external auditors each year,
and submit the ompleted audits through the Central B1k to
the Bank not later than few montbs after the close of the
financial year. The status of each borrower's loan account
(interest, outstanding balance, peymets) would be sent to
itis pariodcally.

URUGUAY
FOURTH LIVESTOCK DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Impact of Bank Lending Program (1959-1971)
A. Pasture Improvements and Ranch Management Practices
1.        Before the Plan Agropecuario (PLAN) commenced its development
program in 1961, Uruguay had improved some 128,000 ha of pasture land 1/.
However, most pastures were poorly established and reverted back to dominant
native grass after several years because ranchers did not practice modern
grassland management methods or use sufficient phosphate fertilizer.
2.        With this limited experience, the first ranch development plans
were made in 1960, with considerable caution and "educated guessing" but
with full appreciation of the role of phosphate fertilizer, legumes and
rhizobia. The first Technical Director recruited a core of about 10 to 12
able technicians, all University graduates in Agriculture and some with a
farming background or practising farmers. Intensive in-service training in
all phases of plan preparation and supervision, including economic aspects,
equipped these men to prepare and supervise the first plans made under
Loan 245-UR. Their ranks included specialists in irrigation and machinery.
Some 76 plans were put into operation by 1961.
3.        From a modest beginning, the technical corps of the Plan Agrope-
cuario, now under a fourth foreign Technical Director, has grown to 67,
including three specialists in water and irrigation, three specialists in
farm economics and production records, a specialist extension supervisor,
a specialist machinery adviser, and an experienced seed production officer.
Included are a Deputy Technical Director, five program chiefs, and eight
area chiefs to supervise and coordinate the work of the Regional Technicians.
The majority of the original technicians appointed are still with the PLAN
in senior positions, testifying to the care exercised in their selection and
the esprit de corps that exists in the organization. The output of technical
ranch plans today has reached a figure of about 4,000 per annum.
4.        In addition, technical assistance in all aspects of pasture and
animal production is provided to all financially-assisted farmers as well
as to others requesting this service. The PLAN, in fact, provides the
1/   1961 census.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
only organized extension service in this field in Uruguay, providing a
nucleus for extension activities that are now being developed in dairy
beef and pigs and may, in future, cater for mixed cropping with livestock.
5.        Complex pasture seed mixtures were used in early ranch plans to
obtain information on ecotypes adapted to the varied pasture environments. By
June 1965, Loan 245-UR, for US$7 million, had been disbursed, providing
technical and financial assistance to 1,446 farmers to improve 130,000 ha and
making available seed and fertilizer (and considerable technical assistance)
for the improvement of 100,000 ha by large farmers who were not permitted to
borrow under the Program. The experience gained under the first loan, along
with the extensive experimentation carried out, and the collaborative trials,
particularly with La Estanzuela Research Center, resulted in the solution
of the main technical problems. By the end of 1964, tae role and economy
of conventional sowing, sodseeding, special purpose pastures, mixed sowing
(with cereals), topdressing and oversowing, including aerial sowing, refer-
tilization, pelleting of legume seed, and the prime importance of adequate
and specific strains of rhizobia, were fully understood. Modern machinery
and equipment had been imported and put to efficient use.
laoculum
6.        Many early pasture failures were traced to nonviable or inef-
ficient inoculum. Under the guidance and work of an expert seconded to
the Plan by FAO, these problems were solved and the selection of suitable
specific strains of rhizobia and the local manufacture of inoculum were
initiated. Today, Uruguay is a leader in this important field in South
America and exports inoculum to neighboring countries.
Silage and gaymakinB
7.        Conservation of surplus pasture in spring and early summer
for winter use was ar. early recommendation but adoption by farmers has been
slow. Silage making is not popular, largely because of labor involved in
"feeding-out", and seii-feeding has noc been adequately demonstrated.  Hay-
making is preferred for pasture conservation. The greater use of hay and/or
silage is being encouraged by the Technical Director. A new type of hay
baler, now available in Argentina, which makes and transports 1-ton bales that
can be placed and covered in a cornier of the hay paddock, is of special
interest to Uruguay.
rtilizer_Us.ige
The early recommendations on fertilizer use have been justified by
subsequent research. Extensive field trials, in progress (by La Estanquela,
tine Plan and other agencies), will enable refinements to be made in recom-
mendations on pasture establishment, management, fertilizer requirements,
pasture utilization, and mineral deficiencies for the various ecological
zones.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
9.        Phosphate (P20 ) usage increased from about 11,000 tons in 1960 to
about 49,000 tons in 1978, stimulated by the provision of credit and sub-
sidies (valued at US$2.4 million). Nine special airplanes for aerial sowing
and four modern bulk trucks have been imported through the PLAN and, in 1970,
some 30,000 ha were oversown by plane.
Animal Production
10.       All avenues of animal production (except poultry) in Uruguay
are sponsored by financial and/or technical assistance through the PLAN.
Improved techniques, especially modern efficient beef breeding and
production methods, are being widely and successfully promulgated by PLAN
technicians. Animal health is good under improved nutritional levels and
diagnostic services are being organized.
Pasture Production
11.       Only a small percentage (15%) of pasture and land improvement
has been directed toward dairy production. The main effort has been to
increase beef production and improve breeding efficiency in both sheep and
cattle. Before improvement, production from natural pasture is estimated
at 70 to 75 kg of beef (liveweight)/ha. Although exceptional yields of
1,000 kg/ha (beef liveweight) are reported, the fertilization of natural
grasslands is estimated to produce about 180 kg/ha; oversowing and sodseeding,
about 220 kg/ha; and conventional pastures, about 400 kg/ha.
12.       While conventional pastures have a high initial production, this
cannot be maintained without good grassland management. The typical pattern
shows decline after two or three years due to invasion by native species
resulting after a few years, in a stable level of production, about 3 to 3-1/2
times that of natural pasture. Sodseeded, oversown, and topdressed natural
pastures with refertilization and good management improve gradually to a
similar level. 220 to 230 kg (liveweight)/ha is a conservative estimate of
average production from improved pastures. This is the estimate used in
deriving the representative models used in the appraisal report for the
Fourth Project.
13.       Certification.  Under the guidance of an FAO expert in seed produc-
tion and processing a seed certification scheme is in operation in Uruguay.
The scheme sponsored by the Plan is administered by La Estanzuela Research
Center.
14.       Processing.  There are two official seed processing plants, the
plant at La Estanzuela with a capacity of 4 tons/8 hrs (financed under
Loan UR-245) and that at the Abastecimientos Agropecuario with a capacity
of 12 rons/8 hrs. Smaller plants are operated by various Cooperatives, and
these smaller plants are of importance to local seed producers and the PLAN
proposes to assist in modernizing these plants where necessary. With the



ANNEX 4
Page 4
existing modern plants available and modest plans for updating and expanding
facilities as required, Uruguay's plans to become self-sufficient in pasture
seed requirements would seem to be a realistic goal.
B. Beef Production and Processing for Export
15.       The effect of the introduction of new technology has begun to
have an impact on beef production for export. During the past three
years, Uruguay has consistently exported more than 100,000 m tons of
beef, reaching 151,000 m tons in 1970.
16.       Since the initiation of the PLAN, some 1.2 million ha of pasture
(about 7% of total area) have been sown with and without PLAN assistance.
17.       In the 10-year period, 1951 to 1960, beef production averaged about
263,000 tons/year.  Following two bad drought years in 1966 and 1967, produc-
tion recovered to 339,000 tons in 1968 and increased to 360,000 tons in
1970. The increase in production during the operation of the PLAN to 1970
is some 90,000 tons/year. If this increase can be attributed to the improvea
production from the 1.2 million ha of improved pastures, it is calculated at
75 kg of meat per ha or 150 kg liveweight per ha. The output from unimproved
natural pasture is estimated at about 70 kg (liveweight)/ha, so that the
total annual output of the improved pastures would be about 220 kg
(liveweight)/ha. This is remarkably close to the average estimated output
of improved pastures, which is about three times that of natural camp, or
some 220 to 230 kg (liveweight)/ha.
18.       If present rates of annual pasture improvement continue (300,000
ha per annum), Government export targets of 220,000 m tons by 1975, worth
nearly US$150 million, could be realized.
C. Income Distribution and Social Effects
19.       The main banefit of the program has been economic -- more beef
for export, thereby substantially aiding the balance of payments. The
Government, however, has been acutely aware of the social implication and
the results of its program within agriculture and, more widely, on income
distribution within Uruguay.
Income Redistribution: Agriculture to Urban
20.       Uruguay, after the Second World War, developed the most advanced
income redistribution policy in the Western Hemisphere. Early retirement,
minimum wage laws, free medical services, free education up to and beyond



ANNEX 4
Page 5
university levels, inexpensive subsidized transport, paid compulsory
vacations and related benefits, effectively redistributed wealth from
the agricultural sector to the urban sector. Uruguay's social security
sector, for example, in the early 1960's took receipts and paid benefits
equal to about 12% of GDP--one of the highest ratios in the world. (U.S.
paid 6% of its GDP in such benefits in 1970). Eroded by inflation,
benefits dropped to 6% of GDP in the mid-1960's but by 1969 had begun to
rise again, achieving 7.5% of GDP. By 1969, agricultural income was taxed
over 30% while urban incomes were taxed at about 12%. Although agriculture
is still being taxed as heavily, the structure of the system has changed
so that it stimulates rather than discourages extra production. This has
not only assisted Government in its important stabilization program, but has
also enabled it to raise minimum wages substantially, and begin to restore
the arrears in social security payments, whose benefits were badly eroded
during the severe mid-1960 inflation. Since partial taxation of the extra
production and income arising from increased livestock production contributes
substantially to public revenues, continued production stimulation is vital if
social stability is to be reestablished in Uruguay.
Consistency with Production Goals
21.       To make the redistribution of income objective consistent with
the objective of increased production, a land productivity tax (Spanish
initials IMPROME) is now being introduced. Its effectiveness is largely
untested, however, because it was approved by Parliament only three years
ago (October 1968).
22.       On the basis of farm size alone, IMPROME has the following
general effects:
(a) No tax will be paid by farmers whose gross production
is less than the equivalent of 200 ha of national
average production, and producers holding up to
2,500 ha can daduct the first 200 ha, valued at
national average production levels;
(b) Producers with farms between 200 and 1,000 ha will
pay less through I3PROME than they did under the
old system, which was mainly concerned with export
duties (detracciones);
(c) Fiscal pressure will be approximately equivalent under
the two systems in the 1,000- to 2,500-ha bracket;
(d)  Producers whose ranch sizes are be-ween 2,500 and
5,000 ha will pay more through IMPROME than they
did under the old system; and
(e) Fiscal pressure will reach almost confiscatory levels
for ranches over 5,000 ha.



ANNEX 4
Page 6
23.       Tt is evident that IMPROME was designed to have a land redistribu-
tion effect, giving the new tax social and economic as well as fiscal pur-
noses. It also encourages efficient producers because production per hectare
al,ove the assumed figure Roes untaxed--the greater the production over the
assumed figure, the greater the untaxed margin. Purthermore, since the land
nroductivitv tax must be naid by all farmers under the assumption that they
all  ro4ouce wool, beef and mutton (wbm), the system encourages more intensive
farming, such as crons, dairy, nork and poultry. No matter what a farmer
really oroduces, he pays on a wbm basis, which allows his production in other
areas to go largely untaxed. Since of the country's 16.5 million ha of ag-
ricultural land, hetween 14 and 15 million ha are used for livestock grazing,
this benefit is realized bv a sector of small farmers that occupy between
1.5 and 2 million ha.
Tncome Redistribution: Within the Agricultural Sector
24.       Aside from encouraging the small farmer through this IMPROME tax
svstem, (Zovernment has been pursuing a series of measures under the Bank
lending program that directly affect the incomes of small producers. In
1070, 72% of the 3,600 loans made under the Plan went to farmers holding less
than 5n0 ha, with 34% going to farmers with less than 100 ha. These small
loans (averaging about US'4600 each) are costly in technical services, but
Government considered this an important function of the 'PLAN and provided
funds from the general budget to cover it. In the plan law, Government has
stated that such technical service costs can go up to 10% of loans made.
2-5.      Also, the incomes of smaller farms are subsidized through the
indexing mechanism. Loans to farmers holding less than 200 ha are adjusted
onlv to 507 of the index charge. With inflation at 15 to 20% per year,
this is a significant subsidy.
3uthsidies
'6.       Farmers investing under PATAN auspices receive substantial
sjbsidies (15T) on phosphate fertilizer, paid from earmarked export tax
nroceeds on beef and wool. In this way, part of the export tax receipts
£oes back to the agricultural sector, partly compensating efficient
farmers and nenalizing those who do not invest.
nairv Reef and Pigs
57.       Peginning in 1Q71, the >1TAN has given particular emphasis to
dairv beef and pig production, carried out on farms of less than 100 ha.
This involves intensive livestock production systems by tJruguayan standards.
Bank loan funds should support overseas training programs for the two
snecial ists recentlv hired bv the PLAN to deal with these programs.



ANNEX 4
Page 7
Indirect Benefits
28.       The successful operations of the Plan Agropecuario in the
livestock sector have generated widespread interest in replicating the
PLAN extension system.  For example, a PLAN GRANJERO (small farms program
for fruits and horticulture) has been organized on the PLAN model. Parlia-
ment approved the bill late in-1968, but chairman selection problems did
not allow the program to start functioning until 1970.
29.       Land Reforms.  Emphasis has also been given to the colonization
program and operations of the National Colonization Institute (INC) have
increased in recent years. This Institute is mainly concerned with settlement
projects for landless farmers, but the area colonized has still not reached
significant proportions (255,500 ha settled from 1948 to 1970). The func-
tioning of the Institute, however, has been improved recently and Government
expects it to operate more effectively in future. The PLAN has actively
cooperated with the Institute, and 178 farmers settled under INC sponsorship
received PLAN credit in 1970.
December 9, 1971

Two tooth ewes enter the breeding flock to be mated at about 17-18 months and lamb  at 2 years of age.
Four tooth ewes (maiden) enter the breeding flock to be mated at about 2-1/2 years and lamb  at 3 years of age.
As of December/January (approx.) (where spring laming practised) lanbs -aned at 5-6 mon,ths become hoggets.

ANNEX 6
Page 1
URUGUAY
FOURTH LIVESTOCK DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Beef Production on Dairy Farms
Introduction
1.        Uruguay, in its drive towards increased beef production for export,
has proposed a sub-project directed at the development of 900 small (450 each
year for 2 years) mixed dairy farms of about 100 ha each for dairy beef
production. This subproject would produce beef worth US$900,000 on the
export market. The same output of milk from each farm would be maintained
by fewer cows producing at a higher level. The improved pastures would
remove the need for concentrate feeds, which now constitute nearly
25% of the cost of milk production.
Background
2.        Economic Importance and Production Patterns.  Dairying is an
important sector of Uruguayan agriculture, contributing about 16% of the
country's total agricultural output, which is equivalent to the contribution
from wool. On the 79,193 farms of over 1 ha in Uruguay, over 8,000 are com-
mercial dairy farms; 60% are under 100 ha.  The average dairy farm is 103 ha.
3.        In 1970, milk production was 740 million liters, of which 450
million were consumed as fresh milk; 280 million were processed into such
products as butters, butter oil, cheese, ice cream, duice de leche, yogurt,
casein, and dry milk powder; and 10 million were used on the farm for calf
and pig feeding. About 70% of the milk is produced in the spring and fall
of the year.
4.        The main milk production area is concentrated around Montevideo
in the Departments of Canelones, San Jose, Florida and Colonia (Map 3).
Herd size is about 50 to 60 animals except in Florida where the average herd
is about 100 animals. Smaller quantities of milk are produced in the De-
partments of Maldonado, Durazno, Soriano and Paysandu.
5.        The national dairy herd is comprised of some half million animals,
of whic'c about 60% are dairy cows and in calf heifers. About 60% of dairy
cattle are Friesians 1/ of good dairy merit. Reasonable levels of production
are attained under good feeding and management (4,000 l/annum compared to
low production level (1,500 1/annum) on the average dairy farm).
I/   The remainder are Normandy, 7.7%; Shorthorn, 4.5%; crossbreeds and
others, 29.6% (source: O.P.Y.A., 1966).



ANNEX 6
Page 2
6.        A comparison between 10 dairy farms with 30Z of pastures improved
under the Plan Agropecuario and 10 typical unimproved dairy farms in the San
Jose district shows an increase in yield from 1,568 1 to 2,314 1/cow and a
decrease in expenditure on concentrate feeds from US$9 to US$3/cow.
7.        Productivity.  Inadequate nutrition is the main reason for low pro-
duction due to overstocking to meet an over-ambitious quota. Concentrate
ration is consequently used in excessive amounts to make up deficiencies
in both the quantity and quality of feed available. Conaprole, the main sup-
plier of concentrate, indicates that it is used by about 97Z of suppliers,
who purchase about 470/kg/cow/annum. Concentrate is used throughout the
year, starting at about 3 kg/cow/day in the spring and increasing througb
summer and autumn to about 4.6 kg/cow/day in the winter. Improved pastures,
as recommended in the Project, greatly reduce this requirement, consequently
resulting in more efficient production.
8.        Forage crops occupy about 28X of the farm area, and are low yield-
ing due to poor cultural techniques and insufficient fertilizer (only about
10Z of crops fertilized). Improved pastures occupy a small part of the farm
(about 8Z), the grazing area being unimproved pastures or, commonly, tumble
down pasture following cropping. Hay and silage-making equipment is rarely
owned, with less than 50% of the dairy farmers making hay and about 12%,
silage.
Husbandry
9.        Dairy farms are labor intensive, 2.6 labor units are employed per
farm, and about 22,000 persons for dairy production. About 97% of the herds
are milked by hand, although there are a few modern milking parlors (herring
bones). There is no set calving pattern; cows calve down all year
round, with a preponderance in autumn. Many cows are milked over an unduly
long lactation and may produce less than two 1/day during winter. Poor man-
agement and nutrition results in lowered calving percentage, a low calving
interval and a high percentage of dry-stock in the herd. On only about 12%
of the dairy farms are the heifers sufficiently developed to calve down be-
fore three years of age, and an average of five lactations per cow is
achieved by only about 50% of dairy farms. Calf rearing is costly since milk
is sometimes fed for six to eight months (about 550 1/calf). As a conse-
quence of the high cost of rearing, only a minimum of calves are home reared,
the rest being sold (for rearing elsewhere but mainly for slaughter as manu-
facturing meat) during their first week.
10.       Marketing and Processing.  The main dairy processor is Conaprole
(National Milk Producers' Cooperative). Each year, Conaprole receives and
processes 250 million liters of milk from 3,150 producers (140 million 1 for
liquid consumption and 110 million 1 for manuf&cturing). Milk produced in
excess of the assigned quota is purchased as manufacturing milk at a lower
price. Farmgate price for quota and manufacturing milk is 31 pesos/1 and
21 pesos/l respectively. Eighteen private dairies process pasteurized fluid
milk for inland cities. However, the major portion of their supply goes to
dairy products for internal consumption and export markets. Over 54% of the



ANNEX 6
Page 3
fluid milk in the country is consumed as raw milk and only 25% (187 million
1) of total milk supply is vasteurtzed.  LarRe ouantiltes of cheese are
still made in small lots on individual farm dairies, particularly when
country roads are impassable.
11.       At present, potential internal demand is considered negligible
in view of Uruguay's current high consumption of dairy products (250 1 of
fluid milk equivalent per capita). Except for slight increases in domestic
consumption due to population growth, additional milk must be processed for
export.
12.       Dairy Product Exports.  The potential for international markets
is uncertain at the moment although the possibilities for exporting dairy
products to other Latin American countries appear to be good. This forecast
is based on recent exports of cheese, butter, butter oil and cream to
Argentina, Brazil, Mexico and the United Kingdom. Export prices of dairy
products have increased significantly since 1968, with the largest increases
occurring during the past five months of 1971. At the beginning of 1969,
surpluses existed that could not be marketed. But, during 1969, Uruguay was
able to find export markets for all surpluses. Value of dairy products ex-
ported was about US$0.9, US$1.5 and US$3.5 million for years 1968, 1969 and
1970 respectively. Since export markets are recent and little information is
available on projected demand for dairy products, a market study is being
organized (para 14).
13.       Pricing Policies.  Until July 1971, Uruguay subsidized the export
of dairy products by giving a reimbursement from the Government budget to the
processor for an additional 20 to 30% of the FOB export value. In July 1971,
Gover ment removed this subsidy and devalued the peso from 248 to 365 per
dollar for such non-traditional exports as dairy products. At the new ex-
change rate, the farm-gate blended milk price (average price for fluid milk
and industrial milk) in Uruguay compares favorably with most other countries.
14.       Market Studies.  Two studies are currently under consideration
by the Uruguayan GoverDment. The first is a short review of the dairy product
export markets in Latin America. Consultant proposals have been invited
and results should be available by October 1971. If the results are positive,
Government intends to commission a detailed review of the dairy processing
industry (public and private) and to develop a Plan of Action for future
investment in the industry.
The Dairy Beef Pilot Sub-Project
15.       The Project would require an investment of about US$2 million,
of which it is estimated the foreign exchange component would be a little over
US$1 million. The benefits accruing would not only be the small but
significant increase in beef production but the introduction of new techno-
logies and the demonstration of efficient milk production, along with
profitable utilization of surplus dairy calves. Dairy farm incomes (for
the typical farm) would rise sharply from US$3,000 before development
to about US$4,500 in the fifth year.

ANNEX 7

URUGUAY
FOURTH LIVESTOCK DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
The Pig Sub-sector and the Hog ProJect Sub-Component
A. The Pig Sub-Sector
1.        The Government is stressing pig production so that pork can be used
as a substitute for beef on the internal market. The plan target was set to
increase production by some 3,800 tons (carcass weight), which it is estimated
would allow an equivalent increase in beef exports. Such an increase would
be valued at some US$2 million in extra foreign exchange. The Project sub-
component is estimated to cost about US$2.3 million with a foreign exchange
cost of about US$1 million.
Production
2.        Pig production has been stable for some years at about 28,000 tons
(liveweight) from a national herd of about 383,000 animals , which contributes
about 4% to the output of the livestock sector. The animals are slaughtered
mainly at heavy weight (120 to 130 kg), over one year old, to supply a local
demand for manufacturing pork, for sausages of various kinds and for a small
output of bacon. A small proportion are killed as "suckling pigs" at a
liveweight under 20 kg. Production is carried out mainly on small farms
under 50 ha located close to the centers of population.
Husbandry
3.        An extensive system of husbandry is commonly employed and pigs are
born and reared in the field, with access to simple shelters. A minimum
of grain is fed to supplement grazing, which is extremely variable in both
quantity and quality. As a consequence of poor husbandry and nutrition,
only one litter of three to five pigs is generally produced and reared per
annum. Under the best extensive conditions, two litters of up to five pigs
each may be achieved, but these are seldom marketed much under one year of
age. The heavy-weight, overfat pigs marketed do not have consumer appeal
as fresh pork. In Uruguay, fresh pork is more expensive than beef, whereas
in developed countries, pork usually costs only 70% of fresh beef price.
Disease and Hygiene
4.        Swine fever and foot-and-mouth disease can cause serious loss
although, under good management conditions, both have been contained and
eradicated by vaccination and hygiene control. Likewise, internal and
external parasites, pulmonary diseases, arthritis, excessive piglet motality,



ANNEX 7
Page 2
are efficiently controlled in those piggeries where modern husbandry tech-
niques are practiced and adequate up-to-date facilities are provided.
B. The 1971 Pilot Program
5.        Modern pig production techniques are known in Uruguay and high
outputs of good quality porkers have been achieved. In 1971, the Plan
Agropecuario financed a pilot program of five units of 50 breeding sows and
10 units of 25, which it estimates will achieve an annual output of some
8,000 quality porkers of 60 to 65 kg liveweight.
6.        Detailed records are being kept for each unit to provide data that
will enable possible refinements to the hog production sub-projects proposed
for the years 1972 and 1973 under the Fourth Livestock Development Project.
Additionally, investigations are being carried out to determine, under
Uruguayan conditions, the extent to which grazing can be used as a substi-
tute for concentrates by the breeding sow.
C. Local Market and Processing Facilities
The Market
7.        During the 1940's, Uruguay exported an average of 3,000 m tons of
pork annually but, following a pronounced downward trend that started in
1950, such exports ceased altogether in 1954. The present per capita level
of donestic consumption is estimated at about 8 kg per annum, almost
entirely as processed pork, compared with 30 kg of pork and pork products
in the U.S.
8.        In 1969, the University of the Republic carried out a survey of
retail butchers to assess the demand for fresh pork on the retail market in
Montevideo. The main conclusions reached were that an extra 22,360 pigs of
the type and weight presently marketed (over 100 kg liveweight) could be ab-
sorbed per annum. An important conclusion reached was that the consumer
clearly prefers pork of lower fat content than is generally available.
Processinu
9.        Four major meat packers slaughtered pigs during 1970 but, due to
lack of domestic demand, only one slaughtered a large number (20,533 head).
Since all pork is consumed locally, small slaughterhouses, which usually do
not have meat inspection services, kill the balance of the pigs processed.
Since little pork is consumed fresh, pork sides are usually sent to small
sausage makers, who cool, bone and process the meat. The present pig pro-
cessing techniques are designed to utilize the whole pig carcasss for
sausage products and the demand for this type of pork meat wlll continue.

ANNEX 11
URUGUAY
FOURTH LIVESTOCK DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Economic Rate of Return Calculation
1.        The economic rate of return of the Project was calculated on the
baels of data shown in Table 1. Key assumptions and adjustments made were:
(a) that the prices of investment and operating cost items would
remain at current price levels except farm workers wages
which are assumed to increase by about 18% over the Project
period in recompense for productivity gains;
(b)  the prices of all outputs would remain at current price
levels;
(c) incremental herd values were credited to the Project at
the end of the life of the investments after 12 years;
(d) all PLAN administrative and technical services provided to
the Project were charged to the Project;
(e) taxes, which would be transfer payments, were not charged to
the Project but fertilizer subsidy is charged.
2.        Based on these assumptions, the economic rate of returr would
be about 30%. Should actual incremental producteon be valued dt 10% less,
due either to a drop in all output prices or a shortfall in physical
production, the economic rate of return would be about 19Z.
Febrary 11, 1972
