URUGUAY
FOURTH LIVESTOCK DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Beef Commodity Review
Major Developments in Beef and Veal Production and Consumption in
Principal Consuming and Producing Regions, 1960-1969
1.        Production of beef and veal in North America and Western Europe,
the world's two leading consumers of these meats, increased 48 percent and
36 percent respectively from 1956-60 to 1970. Expansion of output was par--
particularly rapid in North America during 1963-67 and in the European
Community (EEC) during 1965-68 (Table 1). Per capita consumption of beef
and veal in both regions increased significantly during the 1960's, too:
25 percent in the United States, 20 percent in the EEC, and between 200-400
percent in several Southern European Countries (Table 1). Effective consumer
demand was particularly strong in the EEC, where per capita consumption held
near record levels during the last half of the decade, in spite of sizable
increases in beef prices. 1/ Equivalent gains in cattle prices in England
and Wales during the 1960's undoubtedly contributed to the drop in per
capita consumption of beef and veal which occurred in the United Kingdom
since 1962.
2.        Beef and veal production in the world's three major net exporting
regions (South America, Oceania, and Eastern Europe) increased between 30-70
percent from 1956-60 to 1969 (Table 1). Trends were highly irregular in both
South America and Oceania, however, where output declined significantly for
several years during the middle of the decade before resuming its expansion
during 1968 and 1969. Per capita consumption declined sharply in leading
exporting countries in both regions. This pattern did not apply to Eastern
Europe, where large increases in production yielded supplies sufficient to
double per capita consumption in the leading exporting country, Yugoslavia,
as well as doubling exports from the whole region.
Principal Changes in Volume, Value, ald Directions of Tnternational
Trade in Beef and Veal, 1960-69
3.        Exports of beef and veal meat from 46 selected countries 2/ increasee
96 percent between 1960-69, and in 1969 totalled slightly more than 6 billion
1/   In the Federal Republic of Germany, for example, wholesale prices for
fat cattle during 1967-69 averaged 20 percent higher than in 1962.
2/   Countries in North and South America, Europe, and Oceania, plus the ten
countries in Asia and Africa indicated in Table 2.



ANNEX 12
Page 2
pounds (2.75 million metric tons) on a carcass weight equivalent (CWE)
basis 1/ (Table 2). The growth of this trade during the 1960's was highly
irregular:  exports declined 15 percent during 1964 and 1965 and were only
about 14 percent higher in 1969 than in 1963 (Table 2). Changes in volumes
shipped from South America, the major supplier of imported beef to Europe,
explained nearly all the yearly variations in exports. South American
exporters supplied 40 percent of the net increase in exports which occurred
during the decade, Australia and New Zealand, 18 percent, and Eastern Europe,
11 percent; all the rest was explained by bigger intra-regional trade between
North American and Western European countries.
4.        Most indicators of international prices for beef declined from
1960-63, (concurrently with the fairly rapid expansion in exports which
occurred during these four years) and then rose rapidly from 1963-65
(Table 3), as exports from South America dropped sharply. During 1966-69,
three of these indicators showed divergent trends: Import valuations in
the United Kingdom stabilized following the devaluation.of the pound sterling
in 1967; prices, c.i.f. German ports, declined sharply in 1967 and 1968,
reflecting the combined effects of the implementation of the Common Agricul-
tural Policy in the EEC, the diversion of exports of South American bone-in
beef to the Continent because of temporary bans on such imports by the
United Kingdom, and the sharp increase in beef production in the EEC during
1967 and 1968. Finally, average prices of beef shipped to the United States
continued to rise steadily during the last half of the decade (Table 3).
Comparisons of volumes and values of beef exports during the 1960's indicate
that exportable supplies were fully sufficient to meet current levels of
effective import demand in Europe and the United States during 1961-63, and
also during 1966-68. Supplies were not large enough to meet importers'
requirements in 1964-, 1965, and 1969.
5.        All the net decline in international trade in beef and veal during
1964 and 1965 can be explained by smaller exports fron South America and
smaller imports by the United States, the United Kingdom, and Spain. 2/ The
decline in exports from South America was closely related to the drop in beef
production in Argentina (Table 3). The drop in imports into the United
1/ Bone-in cuts, boneless meat, and cooked, prepared, or canned meat items
converted into dressed weight equivalent, or the weight of the dressed
beef or veal carcass. This conversion excludes offals, animal fats:
exports of live animals -- cattle and calves -- are not included in
these figures, either. This estimate might overstate world trade in
beef and veal meat slightly. Imports in 1969 identifiable by both ori-
gin and destination amounted to only 2.43 million metric tons (Table
2). Current FAO estimates of 1970 net trade balances for most countries
in the world except Asian centrally-planned countries estimate net ex-
ports of 2.65 million metric tons, including CWE of live animals.
2/ Exports by Oceania and imports by the EEC actually increased (Table 2).



ANNEX 12
Page 3
States during 1964 reflected the enactment of the Meat Import Act (PL88-482)
and the sharp rise in beef production that year (Table 1). The further
decline in imports the following year reflected the further increase in
beef production in 1965 and the relative increase in production of cow beef.
Relative import prices favored diverting some beef shipments from the United
States to Western Europe during 1964 and 1965 (Table 3), and this in fact
did occur: imports into the United States dropped 52 percent between 1963-65,
and shipments to the EEC increased 60 percent (Table 1). Strong demand in
Western European markets was confirmed by relatively high import prices in
Both the United Kingdom and Germany (Table 3).
6.        Sharply different trends evolved in the direction and value of
international beef trade during 1966, and several of these developments
persisted through 1969. First, exportable supplies increased sharply
because of sharp gains in output in Argentina (Table 3). Actual exports
from that country increased 53 percent between 1965-69 (on a CWE basis).
Prices for imported frozen and chilled beef in Western Europe came under
considerable pressure during 1966-68 because of larger exports of these
products from Argentina, Uruguay, and Brazil, 1/ and smaller effective
import demand in Western Europe. 2/ Import demand in EEC strengthened during
1969, partly because of the slowing down in the rate of growth of beef
production in Western Europe. Argentine exports of chilled and frozen beef
to the United Kingdon recovered to about their 1964-67 levels, too; and more
South American chilled and frozen beef was exported during 1969 than any
year since 1963 (Table 4), and at much higher prices. 3/
Current Organization and Structure of International Trade in Beef and Veal
Reasons for Persistent Price Instability
7.        Cyclical trends in beef production in Argentina explain nearly all
the yearly variation in representative values for imported beef in Western
Europe during 1963-69 (Table 3). The same inverse relationship between
1/ Exports, on a product weight basis, increased nearly 30% (Table 4).
2/   Which in turn reflected progressively higher import taxes for most beef
and veal imported into the EEC, currency devaluarion in the United King-
dom, the temporary ban on bone-in beef from South American countries by
the United Kingdom during part of 1967 and 1968, and larger beef pro-
duction in Western Europe during 1967 and 1968.
3/   Table 3 indicates average import values for boneless beef and quarters
in the United Kingdom was 40% higher in 1969 than in 1963, and prices,
c.i.f. German ports, for the same kind of carcass beef (Argentine fro-
zen quarters) increased 26% during the same seven years. The differ-
ence in price changes reflects the slight changes which occurred in the
composition of the imported product in the U.K. and the progressively
higher import taxes levied by the EEC on most beef imported for direct
human consumption.



ANNEX 12
Page 4
cattle slaughter or beef production in Argentina and international beef
prices has held true since 1950. Similar fluctuations in beef production in
the United Kingdom and the EEC influenced international beef prices into a
relatively minor extent during the 1960's. Yearly variation in per capita
consumption of beef in Argentina reflected principally changes in beef
supplies (number of cattle slaughtered), although changes in consumer
purchasing power relative to beef prices influenced amounts of beef eaten,
too. Exporters did not compete particularly effectively for available
supplies with domestic consumers of beef in this country: when production
was down, smaller percentages of total supplies were exported, regardless
of high prices prevailing abroad (e.g., 1965 and 1966, Table 3). When
domestic supplies were large, proportionally more was exported, usually
at relatively low prices (e.g., 1963 and 1967, Table 3).
8.        Generally speaking then, changes in exportable supplies of beef in
Argentina explained differences in average prices at which beef was sold in
Western Europe during the 1960's. Exporters in Uruguay and Brazil usually
took advantage of higher prices abroad by exporting more beef during those
years, and they shipped less beef when international prices dropped. This
meant that combined exports from Brazil and Uruguay varied inversely with
Argentinian shipments (table 4). Uruguay usually exported higher portions
of her total beef production during periods of high prices, and proportionally
less during periods when prices abroad were depressed (Table 3). However,
price-stabilizing effects of exports from Uruguay and Brazil were more than
offset by fluctuations in beef and veal production in Western Europe: in both
the United Kingdom and the EEC, production tended to vary directly rather
than inversely with exports from Argentina during most of the decade.
Trade in Fresh, Chilled, and Frozen Beef and Veal
9.        Relatively stable trading patterns in fresh, chilled, and frozen
beef and veal evolved during the 1960's, and these should continue to prevail
during the 1970's. Exporters in Oceania and Central America sold as much
beef and veal to the United States and Canada as they thought they would be
permitted to ship under current American import regulations. Australians
and New Zealanders competed only nominally in Western European markets with
exporters from South America and Eastern Europe, and imports into the EEC
from Oceania were negligible, particularly after the evolution of the Common
Agricultural Policy in 1968 (Table 5).
10.       Competition was quite intense among South Americans and Eastern
Europeans supplying Western European countries during the 1960's, and it was
in Western Europe where the changes in direction of trade occurred. In the
Common Market about half of the increase in total imports between 1963-69
was supplied by other members of the Community. Other European countries
and Latin American exporters shared nearly all the rest of the imports, each
region supplying about half the beef and veal the EEC bought from third
countries (Table 5). Eastern Europe - mainly: Yugoslavia - replaced EFTA
countries as the Community's second most important supplier, principally
because of smaller shipments of beef and veal from Denmark. Total quantities



ANNEX 12
Page 5
of beef and veal imported by EFTA countries did not change very much between
1963 and 1969. European suppliers acquired about half this market, mainly
at the expense of Latin Americans, whose share dropped from about 2/3 to 1/3
during this period (Table 6). Nearly all the beef imported by EFTA countries
was shipped to the United Kingdom, and this market underwent significant
structural changes during the late 1960's. By 1970, practically all British
imports were boneless beef and fresh beef, and Ireland, Argentina, Australia
and New Zealand supplied nearly all this meat (Table 6). Import demand grew
50 percent in Spain and Greece from 1963-69. Exports from Yugoslavia to
Greece and from other Eastern European origins to Spain increased more than
twice as much as did shipments from Argentina and Uruguay to both these
countries (Table 6).
11.       On balance, then, total South American exports of chilled and
frozen beef and veal to Western Europe were about the same in 1963 and 1969,
but the volume of all fresh, chilled, and frozen beef imported by Western
European countries grew nearly 30 percent during these six years. Further-
more, significant changes in the relative importance to the trade of different
suppliers and buyers took place between 1963 and 1969 (Table 4).
Trade in Cooked and Canned Beef
12.       Beef which was cooked and then frozen, canned, or otherwise pre-
served, constituted a sizable share of all beef and veal imported into the
United Kingdom and the Federal Republic of Germany (Table 7). This meat,
on a product weight basis, currently comprises nearly 6 percent of all beef
and veal imported into the United States. Argentinian shipments of cooked
and canned beef doubled fron 1963-65 to 1968-70. About half of these pro-
ducts went to the United States and another 40 percent to Europe. Argentina
now dominates the trade originating in South America. Declines in shipments
from Uruguay and Paraguay since 1965 more than offset the modest increase
in exports from Brazil since 1968.
Major Developments in International Trade in Beef and Veal, 1970-1971
13.       Total beef and veal production in Argentina, Uruguay, and Yugos-
lavia declined more than 6 percent in 1970. All this net decline occurred
in Argentina where the number of cattle slaughtered dropped 8 percent. 1/
Consumer purchasing power in Buenos Aires (relative to beef prices there)
turned up concurrently. Both these events reversed trends which had
persisted for three or four years in Argentina, and cattle prices there
advanced steadily during 1970. Argentinian exports of chilled and frozen
beef dropped 26 percent in 1970, although this decline in shipments was
offset by larger exports from Uruguay and Brazil (Table A). However, exports
from Yugoslavia dropped nearly 40 percent, too. These smaller exportable
supplies were sold in Western Europe at much higher prices during 1970, 2/
1/ Foreign Agriculture Service, USDA, February and July 1971.
2/   The average price for Argentine frozen quarters, c.i.f. German ports,
was up 27 percent in 1970 from 1969.



ANNEX 12
Page 6
in spite of the 4.5 percent increase in beef and veal production in Western
Europe last year. Current estimates suggest that total imports of beef and
veal increased slightly during 1970 in the EEC, and declined elsewhere in
Western Europe (Table 2). Import demand was particularly strong in Italy.
14.       Strong domestic demand in Argentina during 1971 generated a
critical shortage of exportable supplies there, since the number of cattle
slaughtered dropped as much as 40 percent during the first six months,
compared with the corresponding period in 1970. 1/ Exports for the six months
ending June 1971 were half the volume of beef shipped during January-June
1970. Since Yugoslavian exports were down 15 percent and shipments from
Uruguay were off 36 percent, too, representative prices for imported beef
in Western Europe advanced sharply during the first half of 1971. 2/ However,
these prices apparently attracted supplies fron other sources subsequently.
Quotations in July and August were nearly 30 percent below similar values
during April and May.
An Assessment of Uruguay's Competitive Position as an Exporter of Beef
15.       Uruguay's beef cattle industry became more export-oriented during
the 1960's. Domestic per capita consumption of beef and veal declined
significantly after 1965 (Table 1), and progressively larger shares of
domestic production were exported, especially during periods of relatively
high prices (Table 3). Beef and veal production declined rather steadily
between 1964 and 1969, according to estimates which do not include allowances
for live cattle exported (Table 3). Output apparently increased 30 percent
in 1970. 3/ Since exports were lower during the first half of 1971, beef
supplies or processing capacity rather than lack of foreign markets clearly
still limit Uruguay's foreign sales of beef.
16.       Uruguay's competitive position in the chilled and frozen beef
trade in Western Europe improved during the late 1960's. The loss of her
share of the British market (Table 4) was more than compensated by larger
sales and market shares in the EEC and Spain (Tables 5 and 6). Beef exported
from Uruguay usually is sold at prices competitive with similar meat from
1/ Agra Europe, Buenos Aires, August 1971.
2/ At the leading wholesale market in London, similar cuts of Argentine
chilled beef sold for nearly 50% more during April 1971 than average
values during 1970. Average values on exports from Argentina were up
nearly 60%, compared with the year before (Table 8).
3/ USDA, February 1971.



ANNEX 12
Page 7
Argentina (Table 8). However, the meat packing industry in Uruguay has not
yet developed the capability to produce in volume the higher valued products
such as boneless cuts and cooked, frozen beef. 1/ Uruguay's opportunities
for increasing her earnings from beef exports currently are being curtailed
at least as much by lack of appropriate processing capacity as by lack of
beef supplies. This is particularly true in the case of sales to the
United States of cooked and frozen meat and certain other specially prepared
and preserved products. Meat packers in Argentina and Brazil have demonstrated
markets, for these products do exist. In fact these probably are the meat
products for which foreign demand is growing fastest.
Projections of European Net Import Demand for Beef and Veal, 1975 and 1980
17.       Several estimates indicate that net imports by the EEC of beef and
veal, including live cattle and calves, on a CWE basis, increased more than
15 percent a year during most of the last decade (Tables 7 and 9). Net
imports by eleven other European countries 2/ probably declined between
1962 and 1969. (Inter-country differences in statistical treatment of the
CWE of live cattle traded and canned beef imported make estimations of net
imports of this group of countries extremely difficult). Regardless, a
number of recently published projections indicate clearly that net import
requirements in both the EEC and 11 other European countries are expected
to more than double during the 1970's. These conditional forecasts, which
are identified and summarized in Table 9, suggest that net demand for imported
beef and veal by the six member countries of the presently-constituted
European Co'mmunity will grow 9.5 percent a year through 1975, and then will
slow down considerably. Net imports (trade balances) by the other group
of 11 countries are expected to increase well over 15 percent a year during
the entire decade. During the second half of the 1970's, net import
requirements in the latter market are expected to expand more than net
import demand in the EEC. Beef exports from Uruguay stipulated elsewhere
in this report (Annex 1) assumes these shipments (on a CWE basis)
will increase 25 percent between 1970 and 1975. Since European net imports
should more than double by 1975, clearly one can assume that Uruguay will
not lack foreign markets for the incremental beef production generated by
the Fourth Livestock Development Project.
Projections of International Prices for Frozen Beef, 1975-80
18.       Prices for Argentine frozen quarters, c.i.f. German ports, are
projected to vary from $730-895 current US$ per metric ton during 1975-80,
1/ Nearly 70% of Uruguay's beef exports in 1970 was carcass meat. Only
about 30% of Argentina's shipments in 1970 were chilled or frozen
quarters. The rest were products having much higher values (Table 8).
2/   Other European members of OECD except Portugal and Turkey.

and to average $810 during the middle of this period (1977-78). This would
indicate average price gains per year of 2.8 percent since 1965/66, when

during 1963-69 varied from 3.9 percent ot 6.6 percent a year, depending on

19.       Similar, consistent projections of prices for beef exported from
Uruguay during 1975-80 indicate average unit values of $680 per metric ton.
However, the product mix of these beef exports is expected to shift to
higher priced products such as boneless beef, special cuts, and cooked,
frozen beef (Annex 1). These products have individual valuations far
higher than Uruguay's traditional exports of carcass beef (as Table 8 of this
Annex shows). Accordingly, the estimate that f.o.b. prices, Montevideo,
will average $710 per ton during 1971-75 is quite conservative,
